i am building an intelligence engine for my academic coach , i am of the view that the engine itself which comprises of a couple of engines should in total be about 100,000 lines of code at least per module however the whole intelligence engine comprising all the engines is jus a mare 10,000+ , so i want you to analyse and brainstorm 

I’m going through the engine audit against your concern about scale versus actual intelligence depth, then I’ll convert that into a sharper build strategy rather than just a lines-of-code comparison.

I’ve confirmed the current engine is algorithmically real and not toy logic, but it is still mostly a decision-and-orchestration layer, not yet the full cognitive substrate of a world-class academic coach. That distinction is the real issue, not the raw LOC by itself.

Thought for 38s
Yes — and your instinct is directionally right.

From the audit, what you currently have is not “nothing.” It is already a serious decision layer: 6 engines, 53 named algorithms, real persistence, and cross-engine feedback into planning. The total audited code is 12,068 lines, with Topic Intelligence alone at 3,996 lines, plus Coverage, Sequencing, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation engines behind it. 

 


But the deeper truth is this:

12k lines is enough for a strong orchestration brain. It is not enough for the full intelligence civilization you are imagining.

Your current system mainly does:

diagnosis and decision selection,

prioritization and sequencing,

timing and review logic,

risk protection,

adaptation based on past outcomes,

planner feedback. 

 


That is powerful, but it is still mostly a strategy layer.

What you are imagining is much bigger: a coach that can detect obscure causes, form and revise theories, test those theories, understand content deeply, model contradictions between ideas, notice pressure effects, infer hidden weaknesses, compose interventions dynamically, and learn what works per learner over long horizons. Your current audit already hints at some of that through the 3-layer hypothesis system, evidence spine, mastery proof contract, retest logic, risk taxonomy, and adaptation profile, but those are still compact versions of much larger problem spaces. 

 


My assessment
Your engine is currently under-scaled for your ambition, not because 12k is “bad,” but because the intelligence surface you want is far wider than the current engine boundaries.

Right now, the engines are mostly answering:

what topic,

in what order,

with what teaching mode,

at what time,

under what risk,

when to adapt. 

 


But a truly elite academic coach also needs to answer:

what exactly is broken in the student’s cognition, and why?

what hidden variable is corrupting performance?

is the problem knowledge, retrieval, interference, pressure, confidence, misclassification, shallow proof, representation mismatch, or contradictory encoding?

what evidence would falsify our current hypothesis?

what exact intervention sequence should run next?

what should never be shown now because it would destabilize learning?

what content artifact needs to be generated or fetched to test the hypothesis?

what competing explanations remain plausible?

what changed in the learner model after this session?

That gap is the missing mass.

Why it feels too small
The current engine has depth in each area, but each area is still compressed.

Example:
Topic Intelligence has an 18-step pipeline, 7 hypothesis families, 9 teaching modes, a 6-checkpoint proof contract, and a 17-metric evidence spine. That is very good for a first serious engine. But for the kind of coach you are describing, this single engine could easily expand into several distinct internal engines by itself. 


In other words:

You do not have one “too-small engine.”
You have the early nucleus of a much larger intelligence stack.

The real mistake to avoid
The mistake would be to think the answer is simply “make each module 100k LOC.”

That would be a dangerous framing.

A better framing is:

Each current engine should evolve into a family of sub-engines.

So instead of:

Topic Intelligence = one 4k module

You eventually get:

Topic Diagnosis Engine

Hypothesis Competition Engine

Misconception Graph Engine

Evidence Interpretation Engine

Teaching Strategy Engine

Mastery Proof Engine

Contradiction/Interference Engine

Topic Memory Engine

Topic Simulation/Test Engine

That is how you get to 100k+ meaningfully.

What is missing from the current stack
Here is the missing surface area I would add.

1. Diagnostic Inference Engine
This should become far deeper than “7 families of causes.”

It should model:

latent weakness states,

competing explanations,

confidence intervals on each explanation,

what evidence is missing,

what test should be run next to disambiguate,

falsification logic,

hypothesis decay,

hypothesis resurrection.

This is the engine that says:
“Maybe she knows the concept, but only in untimed conditions.”
“Maybe she confuses two nearby concepts when representation changes.”
“Maybe her error rate spikes only after 4 consecutive questions.”
“Maybe the weakness is not topic weakness but stamina collapse.”

This alone could be a very large subsystem.

2. Misconception and Interference Engine
Your current system touches contrast pairs and interference separation, but that is still light relative to the full problem. 


You need a full engine for:

concept collisions,

near-neighbor confusion,

representation interference,

formula contamination,

definition blending,

procedure substitution,

transfer illusions,

contradiction chains.

This engine should maintain a confusion graph, not just pairs.

3. Learner Cognitive State Engine
Right now the system reads performance evidence. Good.
But you also need a durable learner-state model with things like:

pressure response profile,

confidence fragility profile,

recovery speed,

fatigue signature,

hint addiction tendency,

memory half-life by content type,

abstraction tolerance,

response to visual vs verbal vs procedural teaching,

resilience after failure,

transfer elasticity.

Adaptation Intelligence starts pointing there, but it is not yet a complete learner-state engine. 


4. Session Composer Engine
The current engine decides modes and priorities, but you need an engine that actually composes the session:

open with retrieval or not,

whether to stabilize confidence first,

whether to isolate one concept or compare two,

when to inject worked example,

when to switch representation,

when to stop because overload is rising,

when to insert a “proof checkpoint,”

when to pivot into timed pressure,

when to end with a reinforcement window task.

That is different from ranking topics.

5. Content Intelligence Engine
A true coach brain cannot depend only on pre-existing content.

It needs an engine that can:

understand curriculum,

map topics to concept atoms,

fetch or generate notes,

generate discriminative questions,

generate contrast questions,

generate pressure probes,

generate proof tasks,

generate retrieval cards,

identify missing content coverage,

rate content quality,

select content by learner state.

Your risk engine already references content quality lightly, but this needs to become a first-class intelligence layer. 


6. Experimentation Engine
The coach should sometimes behave like a scientist.

It should run controlled probes:

timed vs untimed,

isolated vs mixed,

visual vs symbolic,

worked example first vs independent attempt first,

single concept vs contrast pair,

easy-to-hard vs hard-first,

low-pressure vs pressure-conditioning.

The goal is not just teaching.
The goal is diagnostic experimentation.

7. Long-Horizon Memory Engine
Current timing and adaptation use history, yes. But a real coach needs persistent memory at multiple levels:

topic memory,

misconception memory,

intervention memory,

learner tendency memory,

exam behavior memory,

recovery memory,

parent feedback memory,

content artifact memory.

The engine should remember not just “what happened,” but:

what pattern recurs,

what tends to work after failure,

what destabilizes the learner,

what is slowly improving,

what only appears fixed.

8. Meta-Evaluation Engine
You need an engine that judges the coach itself.

Questions like:

Was the diagnosis correct?

Was the selected teaching mode optimal?

Was the session too early for timing pressure?

Did the planner over-prioritize risk and under-prioritize confidence?

Did the system confuse weak recall with weak understanding?

Was the intervention effective because of the method or because the question got easier?

This is how the coach becomes self-correcting.

9. Content Coverage and Knowledge Graph Engine
Coverage Intelligence is good, but it is currently portfolio-oriented. 


You still need a deeper knowledge graph engine that handles:

curriculum to topic to concept decomposition,

prerequisite strength,

concept neighborhoods,

formula dependencies,

theorem chains,

likely misconception edges,

exam yield mapping,

representation families,

cross-subject bridges.

Without this, some diagnoses remain shallow because the engine’s world model is too flat.

10. Behavioral and Motivation Engine
Academic performance is not only cognition.

The coach needs to reason about:

avoidance,

hesitation,

confidence collapse,

overconfidence,

frustration tolerance,

momentum,

consistency risk,

emotional aftershocks after a bad session,

readiness for challenge escalation.

Your risk and adaptation engines touch this, but only partially. 


My strongest conclusion
You are not suffering from “too few lines.”
You are suffering from compressed ontology.

Too many big intelligence problems are currently packed into a few engines.

That is why it feels too small.

What a more realistic target looks like
I would not say “100k per module.”
I would say:

Current audited stack: ~12k

Serious v2 intelligence stack: 60k–120k

Truly world-class coach brain: 120k–250k+, depending on how much content intelligence, experimentation, memory, and self-evaluation you build

A possible breakdown:

Topic Diagnosis + Hypothesis Lab: 15k–25k

Misconception / Interference Graph: 8k–15k

Learner Cognitive State Model: 8k–15k

Session Composer / Intervention Orchestrator: 10k–18k

Content Intelligence / Generation Selection: 12k–25k

Timing / Reinforcement / Retest OS: 8k–12k

Risk + Protection + Failure Forecasting: 8k–12k

Adaptation + Meta-Learning + Coach Self-Eval: 10k–18k

Coverage + Knowledge Graph + Curriculum Intelligence: 10k–18k

Memory / Analytics / Experimentation / Audit trails: 12k–25k

Now the size matches the ambition.

What I would do next
Do not expand randomly.
Refactor the vision into 4 layers:

Layer A — Perception

evidence ingestion

learner signals

content signals

curriculum signals

parent/teacher signals

Layer B — Understanding

diagnosis

hypothesis competition

learner state modeling

misconception/interference modeling

mastery-state inference

Layer C — Decision

sequencing

timing

risk

adaptation

intervention choice

session composition

Layer D — Learning

outcome evaluation

memory updates

experiment results

strategy performance

coach self-critique

That will expose exactly where the missing code mass should go.

Hard truth
If your dream is a coach that can notice highly non-obvious causes of weakness, test them intelligently, personalize instruction deeply, and continuously get smarter, then yes — 12k for the whole intelligence layer is too small for the final destination.

But it is not a failure.
It is a credible nucleus.

The audit shows the nucleus already has real muscles:

topic hypothesis logic,

proof contracts,

coverage overrides,

timing phases,

multi-class risk,

adaptive profiles,

planner feedback loops. 

 


So the right move is not “rewrite everything.”
The right move is:

split the compressed engines into a larger intelligence lattice and let each hidden cognitive responsibility become its own subsystem.

That is how this becomes a true coach brain.

Thought for 35s
Yes.

And the clean way to do it is this:

keep the 6 current engines as the primary pillars, but expand each one into internal sub-engines, while also introducing a few shared intelligence fabrics that all 6 engines can read from.

That fits your current architecture better than throwing everything into one giant engine. Right now you already have a real 6-engine stack — Topic, Coverage, Sequencing, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation — with cross-engine reads and writes into the planner. 

 


The missing issues I raised can absolutely be covered, but not all of them should live only inside one engine. Some should become shared substrates.

The right shape
Use this structure:

6 primary engines

4 shared fabrics

sub-engines under each primary engine

clear ownership for every intelligence problem

That way, every issue has a home.

The 4 shared fabrics
These should sit beneath all engines.

1. Learner State Fabric
This becomes the durable model of the student.

It stores:

pressure profile

confidence fragility

fatigue signature

recovery pattern

abstraction tolerance

hint dependency tendency

response to visual/procedural/verbal teaching

retention half-life by topic type

transfer elasticity

pace tolerance

persistence after failure

Without this, engines keep making local decisions without a stable learner model.

2. Knowledge Graph Fabric
This is deeper than topic lists.

It stores:

subject → unit → topic → concept atoms

prerequisite edges

misconception edges

contrast/confusion pairs

formula dependencies

representation families

exam blueprint links

transfer relationships

likely bottleneck chains

Your current Topic, Coverage, and Sequencing engines already consume graph-like structures and contrast pairs, but this fabric needs to become much richer and much more central. 


3. Evidence Fabric
This is the truth layer.

It stores:

question-level outcomes

timed vs untimed performance

help usage

retries

transfer outcomes

mixed-context outcomes

delayed recall outcomes

session events

mode used

intervention family used

parent/teacher feedback

self-report signals

behavior during session

Your current evidence spine and normalized topic evidence are the seed of this already. 


4. Content Intelligence Fabric
This supports the coach’s ability to select, generate, test, and refine learning artifacts.

It stores:

curriculum-derived content maps

notes

glossary links

worked examples

misconception drills

contrast sets

pressure probes

transfer probes

mastery proof tasks

retest packs

content quality scores

source provenance

This is the main missing fabric in the current engine audit.

Detailed expansion under every engine
Engine 1 — Topic Intelligence
Your audit already shows this is the deepest engine: topic diagnosis, 3-layer hypotheses, teaching mode selection, mastery proof, evidence spine, and feedback loop closure. 


To meet the wider issues, I would expand it into these sub-engines:

A. Topic Diagnostic Inference Core
Purpose:
Infer why the learner is failing inside a bounded topic.

It should handle:

knowledge deficit

prerequisite weakness

retrieval weakness

misconception

pressure collapse

transfer weakness

confidence/emotional distortion

representation mismatch

procedure-vs-concept split

guessing illusion

accuracy-without-fluency

fluency-without-understanding

Output:

ranked competing hypotheses

confidence per hypothesis

missing evidence required

falsification tests to run next

B. Hypothesis Competition Engine
This is the extension of your existing 3-layer hypothesis system. 


It should:

keep multiple explanations alive

decay weak explanations over time

revive explanations when new evidence appears

distinguish primary cause from co-causes

assign “untested,” “supported,” “contradicted,” “stale,” “dominant” states

This is how the coach stops locking onto the first explanation.

C. Misconception and Interference Engine
This should go far beyond simple confusion pairs.

It should model:

near-neighbor confusion

concept blending

formula contamination

rule substitution

definition interference

symbol collision

same-looking-different-meaning traps

old-learning-vs-new-learning collision

Output:

confusion graph

interference severity

concepts that should not be taught adjacently

contrast requirements

spacing requirements

D. Representation Diagnosis Engine
This handles cases where the learner knows the idea in one form but fails in another.

It should test:

words

symbols

diagrams

tables

graphs

story problems

formal language

reverse-form questions

Output:

representation-specific mastery profile

representation gap flags

recommended teaching representation order

E. Topic Session Composer
This is the missing piece between diagnosis and actual learning design.

It decides:

how the topic session opens

whether to rebuild confidence first

whether to roll back prerequisite first

whether to use worked example first

whether to use contrast before practice

whether to inject timed work now or later

what order the micro-steps should run in

when to stop and bank a win

Output:

ordered topic micro-plan

step-by-step intervention script

evidence expectations for each step

F. Mastery Proof Engine
Your current proof contract is strong but still compact. 


Expand it to evaluate:

explain

identify

compute

justify

transfer

compare

recall later

use under mixed context

use under pressure

recover after error

choose correct method

detect wrong method

Output:

proof profile by dimension

“mastered,” “fragile,” “partial,” “false mastery,” “pressure-unsafe,” “transfer-unsafe”

G. Topic Experiment Runner
This is where the coach acts like a scientist.

It runs controlled probes such as:

timed vs untimed

isolated vs mixed

recognition vs generation

scaffolded vs independent

visual vs symbolic

low-pressure vs high-pressure

immediate recall vs delayed recall

Output:

hypothesis disambiguation results

experiment confidence

recommended next intervention family

Engine 2 — Coverage Intelligence
Your current Coverage engine handles bucket classification, portfolio allocation, gap detection, review obligations, time budget, and trajectory projection. 


To meet the missing issues, expand it into:

A. Curriculum Coverage Mapper
Tracks:

what curriculum units exist

what topics are in scope

what concepts are still unmapped

what content the system still lacks

This prevents “invisible gaps.”

B. Exam Blueprint Coverage Engine
Tracks:

what the exam tends to ask

how often concept families appear

what high-yield structures remain uncovered

whether coverage reflects exam reality, not just curriculum completeness

C. Knowledge Debt Engine
Measures:

hidden prerequisite debt

unresolved concept debt

unstable gains

weak transfer debt

speed debt

proof debt

This should become more explicit than the current gap types.

D. Content Sufficiency Engine
Asks:

do we have enough notes?

enough questions?

enough contrast drills?

enough pressure probes?

enough transfer tasks?

enough examples for this topic?

This is the bridge to the content fabric.

E. Coverage Quality Engine
Not all coverage is equal.

It should rate:

shallow exposure

procedural-only coverage

coverage without retrieval

coverage without transfer

coverage without pressure conditioning

over-reviewed but under-proved topics

F. Portfolio Strategy Engine
Your current portfolio allocator can be expanded into:

rescue portfolio

repair portfolio

acceleration portfolio

maintenance portfolio

exam rescue portfolio

confidence recovery portfolio

This makes coverage strategy emotionally aware, not just academically aware.

Engine 3 — Sequencing Intelligence
Your current Sequencing engine already chooses among 7 models, learns from failed models, applies interference separation, and monitors churn. 


To deepen it:

A. Prerequisite Path Planner
Not just “dependency first,” but:

shortest recovery path

highest downstream unlock path

safest recovery path

lowest frustration path

highest exam-yield unlock path

B. Confidence-Aware Sequence Engine
This decides whether the learner needs:

a quick win first

a stabilizer topic first

a recovery topic before bottleneck repair

no new difficulty until confidence repairs

C. Interference-Spaced Sequencer
This should use the full confusion graph, not just pair gaps.

It should:

avoid collision chains

separate cousin concepts

insert stabilizer topics

schedule explicit contrast windows

prevent two fragile concepts from sitting back-to-back

D. Sequence Simulation Engine
Before committing, simulate:

likely gain

likely fatigue

likely frustration

likely confusion carryover

time cost

exam impact

Then rank candidate sequences.

E. Mode-Compatible Sequencer
Sequence should depend on current teaching mode and learner state:

if learner is fragile, sequence for recovery

if learner is exam-near, sequence for yield

if learner is interference-prone, sequence for separation

if learner is confident but slow, sequence for fluency conditioning

F. Pivot Governance Engine
You already have pivot conditions. Expand them into:

when to persist despite discomfort

when to pivot because the model is wrong

when to pivot because the learner is overloaded

when to lock a sequence for stability

when to reopen diagnostic ordering

Engine 4 — Timing Intelligence
Your current Timing engine already handles phases, reviews, retests, reinforcement windows, daily pacing, and session frequency. 


To meet the wider ambition:

A. Readiness-to-Pressure Engine
Determines:

whether timed work is safe yet

whether speed should be introduced gradually

whether pressure would produce useful evidence or just damage confidence

B. Reinforcement Window Engine
Expand golden windows into:

quick retrieval window

contrast reinforcement window

transfer window

light-pressure window

delayed recall window

consolidation window

C. Recovery Timing Engine
Decides:

when to revisit a failed topic

how long to wait after emotional collapse

when to give an easier related topic first

when to retest immediately versus later

D. Session Rhythm Engine
Decides:

session length

topic count per session

when to mix versus isolate

when to rest

when to schedule high-effort work

when to bank only reinforcement

E. Pressure Conditioning Scheduler
This is critical.

It should schedule:

no-pressure mastery

light-timed exposure

moderate timed drill

mixed timed work

mock fragments

full mock conditioning

This lets the engine diagnose “knows it but breaks under pressure.”

F. Timing Experiment Engine
Runs timing probes such as:

constant timer vs shrinking timer

single-question timing vs section timing

untimed-to-timed transition

time visibility on/off

pressure after success vs pressure after struggle

Engine 5 — Risk Intelligence
Your current Risk engine is already serious: 12 risk classes, 8 compound patterns, early warnings, velocity, and trend persistence. 


To cover the missing issues, expand it into:

A. Cognitive Risk Engine
Tracks:

false mastery risk

interference risk

prerequisite collapse risk

retrieval instability risk

shallow-transfer risk

representation fragility risk

B. Emotional and Behavioral Risk Engine
Tracks:

confidence collapse

disengagement spiral

avoidance pattern

hesitation amplification

learned helplessness tendencies

overload/burnout risk

C. Plan Integrity Risk Engine
Tracks:

strategy drift

over-switching

under-review

over-review

content mismatch

exam misalignment

repair debt accumulation

D. Pressure Catastrophe Engine
This is specific to exam failure under speed/tension.

It should detect:

accurate but too slow

stable in drills but unstable in mocks

collapses only under clock

clock-induced method switching

panic-driven answer degradation

E. Risk Forecasting Engine
Projects:

what gets worse if nothing changes

what can be safely deferred

what becomes catastrophic near exam day

what risks are compounding invisibly

F. Protection Policy Engine
This converts risk into action:

stop new learning

reduce difficulty

schedule confidence repair

force review

block pressure mode

insert recovery topic

isolate interference pair

escalate to parent alert if repeated disengagement

Engine 6 — Adaptation Intelligence
Your current Adaptation engine already tracks trigger conditions, velocity, cross-topic patterns, learner adaptive profile, dwell times, and stop lists. 


This is the best place to absorb several of the missing issues.

A. Learner Model Updater
Consumes all recent evidence and updates:

preferred intervention families

failure signatures

pressure response model

confidence support need

best session length

best mode by topic type

recovery speed

B. Intervention Policy Learner
Learns:

what intervention works for which topic pattern

what never works for this student

what works only before pressure mode

what works only after contrast repair

what works only for abstract topics vs procedural topics

C. Meta-Evaluation Engine
This judges the coach itself.

It asks:

was diagnosis correct?

was sequencing helpful?

was timing premature?

was the selected mode effective?

did the confidence support help?

did the experiment actually disambiguate the cause?

This is one of the biggest missing pieces.

D. Experiment Governance Engine
Keeps a registry of probes run and avoids random experimentation.

It decides:

which uncertainty is worth testing

what experiment should run next

what not to test because learner is too fragile

how much evidence is enough before concluding

E. Strategy Revision Engine
When evidence changes, it decides whether to:

refine

reroute

reopen diagnosis

compress

protect

escalate

lock strategy for stability

F. Long-Horizon Learning Engine
This is the system memory for coaching effectiveness.

It tracks:

recurring weakness patterns

recurring rescue patterns

slow-burn improvements

false recoveries

seasonal/exam-period behavior

subject-specific response differences

G. Coach Self-Critique Engine
Produces:

what the coach is overusing

where it is under-diagnosing

where it is reacting too fast

where it is waiting too long

where content quality is masking diagnosis failure

My recommendation on implementation order
Do not expand all 6 at once.

Because your current engine already has a strong Topic core, evidence spine, proof contract, risk layer, timing layer, and adaptation layer, the best sequence is:

Phase 1
expand Topic Intelligence

expand Adaptation Intelligence

introduce Learner State Fabric

introduce richer Evidence Fabric

This gives the brain deeper reasoning first. Your current Topic and Adaptation engines are already the best launch points for this. 

 


Phase 2
expand Sequencing Intelligence

expand Timing Intelligence

introduce Experiment Governance

introduce pressure-conditioning logic

This makes the brain behave intelligently across sessions, not just inside a topic.

Phase 3
expand Coverage Intelligence

introduce Knowledge Graph Fabric

introduce Content Intelligence Fabric

build coverage-quality and content-sufficiency logic

This makes the coach robust at scale across curriculum and exams.

Phase 4
deepen Risk Intelligence

add forecasting, catastrophe detection, and behavioral protection rules

add coach self-critique and self-audit loops

This makes the system safer and more self-correcting.

Thought for 21s
Yes. Below is the build-grade expansion map.

I am treating your current audited 6-engine system as the nucleus: Topic Intelligence, Coverage, Sequencing, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation, totaling 12,068 lines, with real algorithms and planner integration already in place. 

 The goal here is not to discard that work, but to open each engine up into a much larger internal intelligence structure.

1. Core principle
Your current engine already does genuine reasoning:

Topic Intelligence already has bounded-topic diagnosis, 7 hypothesis families, 9 teaching modes, a 6-checkpoint mastery proof contract, and a 17-metric evidence spine. 


Coverage already classifies topics into 7 buckets, detects 8 gap types, computes review obligations, and estimates time budgets. 


Sequencing already runs 7 model competition with historical penalties and interference spacing. 


Timing already governs 7 learning phases, golden windows, retests, daily pacing, and session frequency. 


Risk already scores 12 risk classes, 8 compound patterns, and 3 early warning types. 


Adaptation already tracks 9–10 triggers, velocity shifts, cross-topic systemic patterns, and a learner adaptive profile. 


So this next step is not “invent intelligence from scratch.”

It is:
convert a compressed 6-engine brain into a layered intelligence operating system.

2. The structural expansion

Learner State Fabric

Knowledge Graph Fabric

B. Primary engines

Topic Intelligence

Coverage Intelligence

Sequencing Intelligence

Timing Intelligence

Risk Intelligence

Adaptation Intelligence

C. Planner / Orchestrator
Your planner already reads risk/adaptation/timing outputs and boosts ranking accordingly. 

 This should evolve into a formal Coach Decision Orchestrator.

 This fabric formalizes it and makes it queryable by all engines.

3.2 Knowledge Graph Fabric
Purpose: turn curriculum and topic relationships into a real concept graph.

Stores

“do not teach adjacent” warnings

Why needed
Your current Topic Intelligence already consumes clusters, concept edges, contrast pairs, misconceptions, and bounded bridges. 

 Sequencing uses concept edges and contrast pairs for interference spacing. 

 This fabric deepens that into a reusable substrate.

10k–18k

7. Build order
Do not build this in parallel chaos.

Phase 1 — Deepen the brain’s reasoning core
Build:

Learner State Fabric

Evidence Fabric

Topic Diagnostic Inference Core

Hypothesis Competition Engine

Topic Session Composer

Mastery Proof Engine expansion

Learner Model Updater

Reason:
This gives the coach a real mind before it gets more planner complexity.

Phase 2 — Make the coach scientific
Build:

Topic Experiment Runner

Experiment Registry Fabric

Experiment Governance Engine

Representation Diagnosis Engine

Pressure Conditioning Scheduler

Reason:
This is where the coach starts testing causes instead of guessing them.

Phase 3 — Make the coach stable across time
Build:

Recovery Timing Engine

Long-Horizon Learning Engine

Sequence Simulation Engine

Pivot Governance Engine

Risk Forecasting Engine

Reason:
Now the system stops behaving session-by-session and becomes truly strategic.

Phase 4 — Make the coach curriculum- and exam-complete
Build:

Knowledge Graph Fabric

Curriculum Coverage Mapper

Exam Blueprint Coverage Engine

Content Sufficiency Engine

Coverage Quality Engine

Content Intelligence Fabric

Reason:
Now the brain can scale across subjects and exams without blind spots.

Phase 5 — Make the coach self-correcting
Build:

Meta-Evaluation Engine

Coach Self-Critique Engine

Protection Policy Engine

Plan Integrity Risk Engine

Reason:
Now the coach is able to critique and improve itself.

9. Bottom line
Yes — we can absolutely provide full details under every engine to cover every issue I raised.

And the architecture answer is now clear:

keep the 6 engines,

give each engine internal organs,

add shared fabrics beneath them,

move from local heuristics to durable learner-state reasoning,

add experimentation,

add self-critique,

add content sufficiency and graph intelligence,

let the planner orchestrate the outputs.

Your current 12,068-line audited system is a strong nucleus. 


But the full intelligence engine you are envisioning is not a 12k system. It is a multi-layer, 150k+ intelligence operating system.

The next best step is to define this one level deeper as a proper spec package:
for each engine: data models, state machines, function responsibilities, trigger conditions, decision contracts, and exact input/output schemas.


proceed

I’m moving one layer deeper now: from engine families into implementation-grade contracts — states, triggers, inputs, outputs, and core function responsibilities. After that, you’ll have something much closer to a real spec scaffold than a brainstorm.

Thought for 28s
Good. Now we move from engine families to implementation contracts.

Your current audited stack already has the right skeleton: Topic Intelligence as the deepest per-topic engine, Coverage as portfolio control, Sequencing as order logic, Timing as when-logic, Risk as protection, and Adaptation as meta-learning, all tied back into the planner. 

 


What it still lacks is a formal operating contract for each engine:

what state it owns,

what events wake it up,

what it must read,

what it is allowed to change,

what confidence it must attach to its outputs,

what invariants it must never violate.

That is what I am defining below.

1. The universal engine contract
Every engine should implement the same top-level contract.

1.1 Engine responsibility model
Each engine should explicitly declare:

Mission: the question this engine exists to answer

Scope: what level it operates on

Read set: what state/tables/fabrics it can consume

Write set: what state/tables/artifacts it is allowed to produce

Trigger events: what wakes it up

Decision contract: what shape of answer it must return

Confidence contract: how sure it is, and why

Protection rules: when it must defer, escalate, or refuse to act

Without this, engines become large functions rather than reliable intelligence components.

1.2 Standard engine envelope
Every engine should receive a standard envelope like this:

type EngineRequest<TContext> = {
  learner_id: string
  subject_id?: string
  topic_id?: string
  mission_id?: string
  session_id?: string
  trigger: TriggerEvent
  context: TContext
  now: string
  trace_id: string
}
And return:

type EngineResponse<TDecision> = {
  engine: string
  status: "ok" | "defer" | "blocked" | "escalate"
  confidence: number            // 0.0 - 1.0
  confidence_reason: string[]
  assumptions: string[]
  missing_evidence: string[]
  decision: TDecision
  side_effects: ProposedWrite[]
  warnings: string[]
  expires_at?: string
}
This one move alone will make the system far easier to reason about.

1.3 Shared trigger taxonomy
All engines should react to a controlled event taxonomy.

type TriggerEvent =
  | "question_answered"
  | "session_completed"
  | "topic_retest_due"
  | "delayed_recall_due"
  | "phase_checkpoint_reached"
  | "exam_proximity_changed"
  | "topic_regressed"
  | "topic_improved"
  | "risk_threshold_crossed"
  | "adaptation_threshold_crossed"
  | "new_content_ingested"
  | "curriculum_updated"
  | "planner_requested"
  | "manual_parent_feedback"
  | "manual_teacher_feedback"
  | "system_recompute"
Your current engines are already partly event-driven in practice. For example, Topic closes its feedback loop after clinic sessions and Timing schedules recalls/retests as things become due. 


1.4 Shared decision confidence contract
Every engine decision should expose:

type ConfidenceBundle = {
  overall_confidence: number
  evidence_volume: number
  evidence_recency: number
  evidence_consistency: number
  historical_support: number
  competing_explanations: number
  missing_critical_data: boolean
}
Why this matters:
your current Topic engine already adjusts hypotheses using evidence spine and intervention memory, which means confidence is already conceptually central. It just needs to become explicit and universal. 


2. Global state machines
Before engine-specific contracts, define the state machines that all engines agree on.

2.1 Topic mastery state machine
unseen
  -> exposed
  -> emerging
  -> partial
  -> fragile
  -> stable
  -> robust
  -> exam_ready
Possible backward transitions:

stable -> fragile

robust -> partial

exam_ready -> fragile

Flags that can coexist:

false_mastery_suspect

transfer_unsafe

pressure_unsafe

delayed_recall_due

proof_blocked

This fits your current proof-aware system better than using one flat readiness number, because Coverage, Risk, Timing, and Adaptation all need richer topic states than just “weak/OK/strong.” Your current Coverage engine already treats unmet proof, false mastery, speed gaps, and prerequisite chains as distinct coverage conditions. 


2.2 Hypothesis state machine
proposed
  -> supported
  -> dominant
  -> retired

proposed
  -> contradicted
  -> stale
  -> revived
Coexistence rule:

multiple supported hypotheses may coexist

only one dominant hypothesis at a time

dominant can be downgraded if contradictory evidence appears

This extends your current 3-layer hypothesis system into a persistent causal model instead of a one-pass ranking. 


2.3 Learner readiness state machine
protected
  -> stabilizing
  -> productive
  -> stretch_ready
  -> pressure_ready
  -> mock_ready
Backward transitions allowed when:

confidence collapses,

regression rises,

risk crosses threshold,

recall debt compounds.

Timing and Risk should both be able to move this state backward. Your current Timing engine already manages phase transitions and readiness for timed work and mocks, while Risk can force protection logic into planning. 


2.4 Sequence lock state machine
open
  -> soft_locked
  -> locked
  -> pivot_review
  -> open
This formalizes what your current Sequencing engine hints at with churn warnings and advice to lock a model for 3 days. 


2.5 Adaptation action state machine
observe
  -> suggest
  -> approved
  -> active
  -> dwell
  -> evaluate
  -> retained | reverted | revised
This prevents chaotic over-adaptation. Your current Adaptation engine already tracks dwell times and filters recommendations when over-adapting. 


3. Engine 1 contract — Topic Intelligence
Your current Topic engine is already the deepest engine, with an 18-step per-topic pipeline, 7 hypothesis families, 9 teaching modes, a 6-checkpoint proof contract, evidence spine aggregation, and a hypothesis-test-verify loop. 


This should become the local reasoning brain.

3.1 Mission
Answer:

What is wrong in this topic, why is it wrong, what should happen next inside this topic, and what proof would count as real mastery?

3.2 Scope
Operates on:

one learner,

one topic,

bounded neighboring concept graph,

relevant prerequisites and confusion neighbors.

Not allowed to do:

full-subject portfolio decisions,

study-time allocation across subjects,

global plan compression.

Those belong elsewhere.

3.3 Internal sub-engines
3.3.1 Topic Diagnostic Inference Core
Owns:

causal diagnosis inside a topic

Input:

concept states

evidence bundle

intervention memory

misconception graph

timing context

learner state

Output:

ranked causal hypotheses

evidence sufficiency

unresolved uncertainties

Core function contract:

infer_topic_causes(topic_case, evidence, learner_state): CauseHypothesis[]
Invariant:

must return at least 1 primary explanation and 0..n co-causes

must never emit a dominant hypothesis if evidence sufficiency is too low

3.3.2 Hypothesis Competition Engine
Owns:

maintenance of multiple live explanations

Core decisions:

promote

demote

retire

revive

request disambiguation probe

Function:

update_hypothesis_states(topic_id, hypotheses, new_evidence): HypothesisLedger
Invariant:

dominant hypothesis cannot persist if repeated disconfirming evidence arrives

3.3.3 Misconception / Interference Engine
Owns:

local confusion graph

Function:

build_topic_confusion_graph(topic_id, question_errors, misconception_edges): ConfusionGraph
Outputs:

confusion pairs

contamination risk

“do not co-teach” flags

required contrast tasks

3.3.4 Representation Diagnosis Engine
Owns:

form-specific understanding map

Function:

score_representation_mastery(topic_id, evidence): RepresentationProfile
Representations:

verbal

symbolic

visual

tabular

applied word problem

reverse mapping

mixed-context

3.3.5 Topic Session Composer
Owns:

micro-plan for the topic session

Function:

compose_topic_session(
  topic_case,
  dominant_hypothesis,
  learner_state,
  timing_context
): SessionBlueprint
Outputs:

entry step

teaching arc

question arc

confidence supports

stop conditions

proof checkpoints

fallback branch

This is not just “choose a mode.”
It is “generate the teachable path.”

3.3.6 Mastery Proof Engine
Owns:

proof contract evaluation

Your current proof contract already checks explain, independent solve, transfer, delayed recall, mixed context, and light pressure. 


Expanded function:

evaluate_mastery_proof(topic_id, evidence): ProofContractResult
Output dimensions:

explain

independent solve

justify

transfer

discriminate from similar concept

delayed recall

mixed context

light pressure

recovery after error

3.3.7 Topic Experiment Runner
Owns:

disambiguation probes

Function:

run_topic_probe(topic_id, probe_type, learner_state): ProbePlan
Probe types:

timed_vs_untimed

isolated_vs_mixed

scaffolded_vs_independent

visual_vs_symbolic

retrieval_vs_recognition

contrast_before_practice_vs_after

3.4 Topic Intelligence decision artifact
type TopicDecision = {
  topic_id: string
  dominant_hypothesis: string
  co_causes: string[]
  topic_state: TopicState
  teaching_mode: string
  entry_point: string

3.5 Topic engine trigger rules
Wake this engine when:

a topic session ends

a topic regresses

new evidence arrives

retest completes

delayed recall completes

misconception spike detected

planner requests topic decision refresh

That fits your current feedback loop closure model directly. 


3.6 Topic engine non-negotiable invariants
never mark advance_ready if proof contract is incomplete

never select pressure-heavy mode when learner readiness is protected

never treat speed failure as knowledge failure without disambiguation evidence

never collapse multiple live causes into one if evidence is conflicting

never recommend adjacent interference-heavy concepts without explicit contrast strategy

10. The conflict-resolution layer
This is important because once the system gets deeper, engines will disagree.

Example:

Sequencing says “fix bottleneck now.”

Timing says “today should be light.”

Risk says “pressure is dangerous.”

Adaptation says “current family is failing.”

Topic says “need contrast repair before anything else.”

You need a formal conflict resolver.

10.1 Conflict resolution order
I would use:

Protection

Readiness

Proof debt

Bottleneck severity

Exam proximity

Portfolio balance

Confidence benefit

Efficiency

This ensures safety and correctness beat convenience.

3. Core domain contract
3.1 Canonical IDs
pub type LearnerId = uuid::Uuid;
pub type SubjectId = uuid::Uuid;
pub type TopicId = uuid::Uuid;
pub type ConceptId = uuid::Uuid;
pub type SessionId = uuid::Uuid;
pub type MissionId = uuid::Uuid;
pub type StrategyId = uuid::Uuid;
pub type ExperimentId = uuid::Uuid;
pub type TraceId = uuid::Uuid;
Do not use raw strings deep in engine logic.

3.2 Universal engine trait
pub trait IntelligenceEngine {
    type Context;
    type Decision;

    fn engine_name(&self) -> &'static str;

    fn handles(&self, event: &TriggerEvent) -> bool;

    fn execute(
        &self,
        req: EngineRequest<Self::Context>,
    ) -> Result<EngineResponse<Self::Decision>, EngineError>;
}
This gives every engine the same top-level execution model.

3.3 Engine request/response
#[derive(Debug, Clone)]
pub struct EngineRequest<TContext> {
    pub learner_id: LearnerId,
    pub subject_id: Option<SubjectId>,
    pub topic_id: Option<TopicId>,
    pub mission_id: Option<MissionId>,
    pub session_id: Option<SessionId>,
    pub trigger: TriggerEvent,
    pub context: TContext,
    pub now: chrono::DateTime<chrono::Utc>,
    pub trace_id: TraceId,
}
#[derive(Debug, Clone)]
pub struct EngineResponse<TDecision> {
    pub engine: EngineName,
    pub status: EngineStatus,
    pub confidence: ConfidenceBundle,
    pub assumptions: Vec<String>,
    pub missing_evidence: Vec<MissingEvidence>,
    pub decision: TDecision,
    pub side_effects: Vec<ProposedWrite>,
    pub warnings: Vec<String>,
    pub expires_at: Option<chrono::DateTime<chrono::Utc>>,
}
#[derive(Debug, Clone, Copy)]
pub enum EngineStatus {
    Ok,
    Defer,
    Blocked,
    Escalate,
}

bitflags::bitflags! {
    pub struct TopicFlags: u32 {
        const FALSE_MASTERY_SUSPECT = 0b00000001;
        const TRANSFER_UNSAFE       = 0b00000010;
        const PRESSURE_UNSAFE       = 0b00000100;
        const DELAYED_RECALL_DUE    = 0b00001000;
        const PROOF_BLOCKED         = 0b00010000;
        const INTERFERENCE_HOT      = 0b00100000;
        const PREREQ_BLOCKED        = 0b01000000;
    }
}
This is important because your current Coverage logic already treats false mastery, proof-blocked topics, speed gaps, and prerequisite chains as different gap types rather than one generic weakness.

5.2 Event envelope
#[derive(Debug, Clone)]
pub struct DomainEvent {
    pub event_id: uuid::Uuid,
    pub learner_id: LearnerId,
    pub subject_id: Option<SubjectId>,
    pub topic_id: Option<TopicId>,
    pub session_id: Option<SessionId>,
    pub event_type: TriggerEvent,
    pub occurred_at: chrono::DateTime<chrono::Utc>,
    pub trace_id: TraceId,
    pub payload: serde_json::Value,
}
5.3 Event bus policy
Use outbox pattern:

domain write commits first

event is inserted in intel_event_outbox

runner consumes and fans out recomputation

engine writes use idempotency key = (engine_name, learner_id, topic_id, trigger, trace_id)

That prevents duplicate cascades.

6. Persistence ownership
This is critical. Engines should not all write everywhere.

7.6 Proof contract
Your current engine already uses the 6 checkpoints of explain, independent solve, transfer, delayed recall, mixed context, and light pressure. Preserve that and add richer dimensions without breaking the current core. 


#[derive(Debug, Clone, Copy, PartialEq, Eq, Hash)]
pub enum ProofDimension {
    Explain,
    IndependentSolve,
    Transfer,
    DelayedRecall,
    MixedContext,
    LightPressure,
    JustifyMethod,
    DiscriminateFromNeighbor,
    RecoverAfterError,
}
#[derive(Debug, Clone)]
pub struct ProofCheckpoint {
    pub dimension: ProofDimension,
    pub required: bool,
    pub met: bool,
    pub confidence: f32,
    pub evidence_count: u32,
}
#[derive(Debug, Clone)]
pub struct ProofContractResult {
    pub topic_id: TopicId,
    pub checkpoints: Vec<ProofCheckpoint>,
    pub advance_ready: bool,
    pub proof_depth_score: f32,
    pub capped_by_evidence: bool,
}
The advance_ready invariant must remain strict, because your current system already treats full proof completion as a prerequisite for genuine advancement.

7.10 Topic engine invariants
These should be enforced in code, not just documentation.

Never mark advance_ready = true if any required proof checkpoint is unmet.

Never choose pressure-heavy work when learner readiness is Protected or Stabilizing.

Never collapse speed failure into knowledge deficit without disambiguation evidence.

Never keep a dominant hypothesis after repeated contradictory evidence.

Never schedule interference-heavy neighbor topics without explicit contrast handling.

These invariants are direct extensions of what your current Topic engine is already doing with proof, evidence, hypotheses, and rerouting.

Your current Adaptation engine already builds a learner adaptive profile that captures what works for this student, including mode effectiveness, velocity, diminishing returns, and stop lists. 

 This should become a central shared fabric instead of staying implicit inside adaptation logic.

10.3 Conflict resolution order
Hard-code the resolution order:

Protection

Readiness

Proof debt

Bottleneck severity

Exam proximity

Portfolio balance

Confidence benefit

Efficiency

This is what keeps the planner from doing something locally clever but globally unsafe.

11. Engine runner flow
11.1 Recommended execution order on session_completed
When a session finishes:

Write raw session result

Emit SessionCompleted

Recompute Topic Intelligence for affected topics

Recompute Timing for recall/retest consequences

Recompute Risk for topic/subject impact

Recompute Adaptation for strategy health update

Recompute Coverage if enough aggregate state changed

Recompute Sequencing if bottlenecks or stability changed

Run Planner

Persist today_plan and session_recommendation

This ordering is consistent with the current audit, where Topic feeds downstream artifacts to Timing, Risk, and Adaptation, and the planner then reads Timing, Risk, and Adaptation outputs.

Do not rewrite the whole engine stack in one shot.

Phase 1
Introduce:

intel-domain

intel-store

canonical enums and state machines

new request/response contracts

No behavior rewrite yet.

Phase 2
Wrap current Topic engine behind new IntelligenceEngine trait.
Map old outputs into:

ProofContractResult

SessionBlueprint

Phase 3
Introduce:

ic_learner_model

ic_topic_hypotheses

ic_topic_proof_contracts

ic_topic_decisions

Populate them from current Topic pipeline.

Phase 4
Wrap Timing, Risk, Adaptation behind new contracts.

Phase 5
Wrap Planner and add conflict logging.

Phase 6
Split Topic into real submodules:

diagnostic inference

hypothesis competition

session composer

proof engine

experiment runner

That sequence gives you structure first, then deeper intelligence.

I’m drilling into Topic Intelligence now as the first full build spec: schema, repositories, execution flow, invariants, and test surface. This is the right place to start because it already has the richest logic and the heaviest downstream impact in your current stack.

Thought for 20s
Below is Spec Pack 2A — Topic Intelligence Build Spec.

It is anchored to your current audited Topic engine, which is already the deepest engine in the system at 3,996 lines, with an 18-step pipeline, 7 hypothesis families, 9 teaching modes, a 6-checkpoint mastery proof contract, a 17-metric evidence spine, and a feedback loop that writes into intervention history and delayed recall scheduling. 


1. Mission
Topic Intelligence owns one question:

For this learner and this topic, what is broken, why is it broken, how should we teach it next, and what proof would count as real mastery?

That matches the current audited role exactly: bounded to a topic graph, not a full subject, and responsible for entry, diagnosis, teaching mode, proof, evidence use, and persistence. 


2. Non-goals
Topic Intelligence must not own:

subject-wide portfolio balancing

global time allocation across subjects

sequencing across the whole subject

global risk protection policy

adaptation pacing across many topics

Those are separate because your current engine graph already uses Topic as a producer for Coverage, Timing, Risk, Adaptation, and then the planner.

8. Read repositories
pub trait TopicReadRepo {
    fn load_topic_scope(&self, learner_id: LearnerId, topic_id: TopicId) -> Result<TopicScope>;
    fn load_concept_states(&self, learner_id: LearnerId, topic_id: TopicId) -> Result<Vec<ConceptState>>;
    fn load_recent_evidence(&self, learner_id: LearnerId, topic_id: TopicId, limit: u32) -> Result<Vec<EvidencePoint>>;
    fn load_recent_responses(&self, learner_id: LearnerId, topic_id: TopicId, limit: u32) -> Result<Vec<ResponseFact>>;
    fn load_intervention_memory(&self, learner_id: LearnerId, topic_id: TopicId) -> Result<InterventionMemory>;
    fn load_tensions(&self, learner_id: LearnerId, topic_id: TopicId) -> Result<TopicTensionContext>;
    fn load_prior_teaching(&self, learner_id: LearnerId, topic_id: TopicId) -> Result<Option<TopicTeachingRow>>;
    fn load_session_blueprint(&self, blueprint_id: Uuid) -> Result<Option<SessionBlueprint>>;
}
These reads line up with the current audited data inputs: mission state, concept nodes, topic clusters, edges, topic profile, compaction state, contrast pairs, misconceptions, evidence points, responses, intervention history, timing decisions, tensions, coaching episodes, session blueprints, and prior topic teaching rows. 


9. Write repositories
pub trait TopicWriteRepo {
    fn upsert_topic_case(&self, row: &TopicCaseV2) -> Result<()>;
    fn replace_topic_hypotheses(&self, learner_id: LearnerId, topic_id: TopicId, rows: &[TopicHypothesis]) -> Result<()>;
    fn upsert_teaching_decision(&self, row: &TopicTeachingRow) -> Result<()>;
    fn append_evidence_points(&self, rows: &[EvidencePoint]) -> Result<()>;
    fn upsert_proof_contract(&self, row: &ProofContractRow) -> Result<()>;
    fn insert_session_blueprint(&self, row: &SessionBlueprintRow) -> Result<()>;
    fn append_intervention_history(&self, row: &InterventionHistoryRow) -> Result<()>;
    fn insert_decision_audit(&self, row: &TopicDecisionAuditRow) -> Result<()>;
}
10. Execution flow
The current engine already has an 18-step analysis pipeline. The build spec should preserve that shape and split each step into explicit modules. 


Stage A — Scope and concept analysis
load_topic_scope()

load_concept_states()

rank_concepts()

find_bottleneck()

detect_false_mastery()

Stage B — Evidence and memory
build_evidence_spine()

load_intervention_memory()

Stage C — Diagnosis
seed_hypotheses()

apply_evidence_feedback()

apply_intervention_feedback()

select_dominant_hypothesis()

Stage D — Teaching choice
select_teaching_mode()

apply_evidence_override()

apply_intervention_reroute()

compute_entry_point()

Stage E — Proof and scheduling
build_proof_contract()

compute_delayed_recall_obligation()

Stage F — Action artifact
build_session_blueprint()

build_strategy_file()

persist_topic_decision()

The audited engine currently describes 18 steps because session artifact generation and persistence are bundled more tightly; I would split them slightly for cleaner ownership without changing the underlying logic.

dependency importance: 0.20

exam leverage: 0.18

misconception risk: 0.15

difficulty: 0.12

transfer importance: 0.10

speed sensitivity: 0.10

confidence sensitivity: 0.10

abstraction level: 0.06

minus compaction penalty up to 0.12

    // pure function

pub fn seed_hypotheses(
    bottleneck: &RankedConcept,

    // produces 1..7 families

The current audited engine already boosts:

prerequisite/knowledge from hint dependency

transfer from low transfer success

retrieval from delayed-recall failures

pressure from timed failures

misconception from recurring misconception evidence

Preserve the current priority cascade, then allow:

11.6 Proof evaluation
The current audited rule is strict:
advance_ready only when all required checkpoints are met and current level is at or above target, with evidence-based capping. 


pub fn evaluate_proof_contract(

12. Evidence ingestion contract
The current engine already ingests coaching episode evidence by normalizing completed coaching steps into weighted evidence points, with flags and hint penalties. Preserve that function almost exactly. 


pub fn ingest_coach_episode_evidence(
    learner_id: LearnerId,
    subject_id: SubjectId,
    topic_id: TopicId,
    episode: &CoachEpisodeFact,
) -> Vec<EvidencePoint>;
Weight policy:

worked example step: low-to-medium weight

independent solve: medium-to-high weight

transfer probe: high weight

delayed recall: high weight

timed probe: high weight for pressure dimension

hints used: halve or sharply reduce weight

repeated identical question: weight decay

13. Entry points
The audited engine already computes five entry-point types. Keep that concept but formalize the enum. 


pub enum SessionEntryPoint {
    DiagnoseFirst,
    RollbackPrerequisite,
    ContrastRepair,
    ConfidenceRecovery,
    ProofCheckpoint,
}
Selection rules:

dominant prerequisite weakness -> RollbackPrerequisite

dominant misconception with hot confusion neighbor -> ContrastRepair

dominant confidence-emotional with high tension -> ConfidenceRecovery

conflicting evidence -> DiagnoseFirst

strong topic with one missing proof dimension -> ProofCheckpoint

14. Session blueprint
pub struct SessionStep {
    pub ordinal: u8,
    pub step_type: SessionStepType,
    pub concept_ids: Vec<ConceptId>,
    pub target_dimension: Option<ProofDimension>,
    pub expected_minutes: u16,
}

pub enum SessionStepType {
    Explain,
    GuidedRepair,
    WorkedExample,
    ContrastDrill,
    IndependentSolve,
    TransferProbe,
    RetrievalProbe,
    TimedProbe,
    Reflection,
}
Blueprint generation rules:

max 5–8 steps

no more than 2 high-load steps back-to-back

if learner is fragile, insert an early win before any timed or mixed-context probe

if misconception is dominant, schedule contrast before independent solve

if pressure-performance is dominant, do not start with timed probe; earn baseline first

15. Feedback loop closure
The current audited function close_topic_clinic_feedback_loop() is one of the most important behaviors in the whole engine: after every clinic session, rerun full topic analysis, resolve intervention family, infer outcome, write history, schedule delayed recall, reload memory. 


Formalize it as:

pub fn close_topic_clinic_feedback_loop(
    learner_id: LearnerId,
    topic_id: TopicId,
    session_id: SessionId,
) -> Result<FeedbackLoopResult>;
Steps:

ingest episode evidence

append evidence points

rerun topic analysis

infer intervention outcome

append intervention history

schedule delayed recall if required

persist new topic teaching row

emit downstream event for Timing, Risk, Adaptation, Planner

16. Trigger handling
Topic Intelligence should wake on:

QuestionAnswered if question belongs to an active coached topic

SessionCompleted

TopicRetestDue after retest result arrives

DelayedRecallDue after delayed recall result arrives

TopicRegressed

TopicImproved

ManualTeacherFeedback

ManualParentFeedback

PlannerRequested

SystemRecompute

Primary hot path:
SessionCompleted -> ingest evidence -> rebuild evidence spine -> rerun diagnosis -> write decision -> emit downstream changes

That is consistent with the current cross-engine integration where Topic outputs feed Coverage, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation. 


17. Invariants
These must be coded as hard assertions or validation errors.

Never mark advance_ready = true if any required proof checkpoint is unmet. This is already a core audited rule. 


Never declare a dominant hypothesis if evidence sufficiency is below threshold.

Never interpret speed failure as knowledge deficit without pressure disambiguation evidence.

Never reroute mode away from the dominant family unless stall criteria are met.

Never schedule delayed recall without a persisted teaching row.

Never overwrite prior hypothesis audit history; append snapshots.

Never let a topic appear stable if false-mastery score remains above threshold.

Never generate a blueprint without at least one measurable expected evidence target.

18. Error classes
pub enum TopicEngineError {
    ScopeLoadFailed,
    ConceptStateMissing,
    InsufficientEvidence,
    InvalidProofContract,
    BlueprintConstructionFailed,
    PersistenceConflict,
    StaleInterventionMemory,
    UnsupportedTrigger,
}
Recovery rules:

InsufficientEvidence -> return EngineStatus::Defer with missing evidence requirements

PersistenceConflict -> safe retry with idempotency key

StaleInterventionMemory -> reload once, then fail hard

19. Tests
Unit tests
concept ranking respects current weights

compaction penalty lowers rank without flipping obviously critical bottlenecks

hint dependency boosts prerequisite and knowledge hypotheses

delayed-recall failure boosts retrieval hypothesis

timed failure boosts pressure-performance hypothesis

recurring misconception tags boost misconception hypothesis

stalled family forces reroute when criteria met

proof contract blocks advancement when one required checkpoint is false

These are all direct reflections of the audited Topic algorithms. 


Integration tests
Prerequisite weakness case

low hint-free performance

weak prerequisite nodes

expected dominant hypothesis = PrerequisiteWeakness

expected mode = PrerequisiteRollback

Misconception case

recurring misconception tags

confusion neighbor present

expected mode = ContrastRepair

Pressure case

untimed success high

timed success low

expected dominant hypothesis = PressurePerformance

expected proof state includes LightPressure = unmet

False mastery case

profile stable

transfer and delayed recall weak

expected false mastery flag true

expected no advance_ready

Stalled intervention case

family attempted 2+ times

effectiveness <50%

expected reroute to next recommended mode

Clinic closure case

session completed

evidence appended

intervention history written

delayed recall row created if needed

Golden end-to-end test
Given:

topic “Osmosis vs Diffusion”

high misconception recurrence

transfer weak

pressure neutral

family history says misconception interventions worked once, confidence mode did not

Expected:

dominant hypothesis = Misconception

mode = ContrastRepair

entry point = ContrastRepair

proof requires DiscriminateNeighbor

blueprint includes contrast drill before independent solve

delayed recall maybe scheduled after session if proof improves but remains fragile

20. Sample end-to-end execution trace
Input

learner: L1

topic: T_osmosis_diffusion

recent accuracy: 0.68

transfer success: 0.29

timed success: 0.64

delayed recall: 0.55

hint dependency: 0.22

recurring misconception tags: “osmosis/diffusion swapped”

intervention memory: misconception family 1 success, confidence family 2 stalls

Flow

scope loaded

concept states loaded

concept ranking identifies “selective membrane” as bottleneck neighbor

false mastery detector raises moderate concern

evidence spine built

hypotheses seeded:

Misconception 0.58

TransferWeakness 0.44

KnowledgeDeficit 0.31

evidence feedback boosts misconception and transfer

intervention feedback penalizes confidence-related branch

dominant hypothesis becomes Misconception

mode cascade suggests ContrastRepair

no evidence override needed

no reroute needed

proof contract shows explain=true, independent_solve=false, transfer=false, mixed_context=false

entry point = ContrastRepair

blueprint produced:

contrast explanation

side-by-side feature drill

sort examples

independent distinction questions

transfer question

teaching row persisted

clinic ends later, closure function writes intervention result and schedules delayed recall

That trace is faithful to the existing audited structure: diagnosis, evidence spine adjustment, intervention-memory adjustment, mode selection, proof construction, persistence, and clinic-loop closure.
