That is the difference between an audited engine and a build-grade engine spec.

The next logical step is Spec Pack 2C — Topic Intelligence Rust interfaces + repository implementations + test fixtures, where this algorithm sheet gets translated into concrete Rust modules, method signatures, and golden test data.


how about describing in great detail , all the sub engines , the role they play , how to get them built . the main engine they belong to 

I’m going back into the engine audit and using it as the anchor while I expand this into a full sub-engine atlas: what each sub-engine does, why it exists, which parent engine owns it, and how to actually build it.

Thought for 59s
Yes. The clean way to do this is to separate what already exists as the main engine layer from what should exist as the internal sub-engine layer.

Your audit already confirms six real parent engines with clear responsibilities: Topic Intelligence is the deepest per-topic engine; Coverage does portfolio-level completeness and time-budget analysis; Sequencing chooses and stabilizes order models; Timing governs phases, reinforcement windows, and retests; Risk scores 12 risk classes plus compound patterns; Adaptation handles trigger-based strategy change, velocity, learner profile, and stop lists. The engines are already integrated through shared writes and planner feedback. 

 


What follows is the expanded sub-engine atlas I would build under each parent engine.

1. Topic Intelligence
Parent role: decide how one topic should be entered, diagnosed, decomposed, taught, proved, and rechecked for one learner. In the audit, it already runs an 18-step pipeline, a 3-layer hypothesis system, 9 teaching modes, a 6-checkpoint proof contract, evidence ingestion, and clinic-loop closure. 


This parent engine should become the largest engine family.

1.1 Topic Scope Loader
This sub-engine defines the local battlefield. It decides which concepts belong to the topic, which prerequisite bridges matter, and which nearby concepts are dangerous enough to include. Its job is to stop the topic engine from reasoning over an overly wide or overly narrow graph.

It should read topic clusters, prerequisite bridges, concept edges, contrast pairs, and misconception links. It should output a bounded topic graph with three zones: core topic concepts, prerequisite repair concepts, and interference neighbors.

To build it, start with deterministic graph extraction only. Do not add heuristics first. Phase 1 is a strict graph loader. Phase 2 adds bridge-pruning rules. Phase 3 adds “interference hot zone” expansion. The success criterion is that every topic analysis begins from a stable, explainable local graph rather than ad hoc joins.

1.2 Concept State Assembler
This sub-engine turns raw concept data into a usable state vector. In the audit, Topic already gathers 16 fields per concept from profile, compaction, edges, and misconceptions. 


Its role is to build a per-concept snapshot containing readiness, fragility, recall, transfer sensitivity, speed sensitivity, misconception exposure, prerequisite depth, exam leverage, and proof depth. This is the raw material for all diagnosis.

To build it, define a ConceptState struct first and keep it purely descriptive. Then implement field calculators one by one. Only after that add derived fields like “dependency pressure” or “confidence sensitivity.” This module should stay embarrassingly inspectable because it is the base truth for later diagnosis.

1.3 Concept Ranker
This sub-engine prioritizes which concept in the local graph deserves attention first. The audited engine already ranks concepts on eight weighted dimensions plus a compaction penalty. 


Its role is not just “find the weakest concept.” It should find the concept with the highest structural damage potential. That means it must combine weakness with downstream blockage, exam leverage, and misconception risk.

To build it, preserve the current weighted ranker as the base implementation. Then add a second pass that computes bottleneck amplification. Then add debug traces showing why one concept outranked another. This should become a pure scoring module with no side effects.

1.4 Bottleneck Detector
This sub-engine asks: which concept is poisoning the rest of the topic? It is different from the ranker because rank gives priority, while bottleneck detection proves structural blockage.

Its role is to identify whether the learner’s failure is local, distributed, or bottleneck-driven. If a single concept blocks five downstream steps, the session should repair that concept first. If weakness is diffuse, the plan should not overcommit to one node.

To build it, implement simple downstream-count logic first. Then add weighted blockage, where high-exam-leverage descendants count more. Then add evidence concentration so it can distinguish “theory bottleneck” from “observed bottleneck.”

1.5 False Mastery Detector
The audit confirms false mastery is already detected inside Topic and reused elsewhere.

Its role is to catch the case where profile scores look stable but transfer, delayed recall, or mixed-context performance say otherwise. This protects the coach from moving on too early.

To build it, first define a rule-based detector: stable profile plus weak transfer/recall equals suspicion. Then add a score. Then add subtype labels such as recall-false-mastery, pressure-false-mastery, and interference-false-mastery. This sub-engine should write a topic flag, not a final decision.

1.6 Evidence Spine Builder
The audit shows Topic already builds a 17-metric evidence spine from normalized topic evidence points and response data. 


Its role is to turn messy learner behavior into structured evidence: recent accuracy, hint dependency, timed success, transfer success, delayed recall success, mixed-context success, speed profile, and recurring misconception density.

To build it, start by formalizing the evidence schema. Then create aggregation functions per metric. Then add recency weighting. Then add contradiction detection so the spine can report “strong in untimed but weak in timed” instead of flattening that signal into an average.

1.7 Hypothesis Seeder
The audit confirms seven current cause families: prerequisite weakness, misconception, transfer weakness, retrieval weakness, pressure performance, knowledge deficit, and confidence/emotional. 


Its role is to generate plausible explanations from concept-state patterns before evidence and intervention memory reshape them. It should always propose multiple candidates, never one.

To build it, hard-code rule templates for each family first. Then convert them into weighted seeders. Then add support for multi-cause topics where two families are jointly plausible. This should output a hypothesis ledger, not a verdict.

1.8 Hypothesis Evidence Adjuster
This is the second layer of the current audited hypothesis system. It increases or decreases confidence based on evidence spine signals such as hint dependency, low transfer, delayed recall failure, timed failure, and recurring misconceptions. 


Its role is to prevent concept-state diagnosis from becoming static. It should let observed behavior overrule structural assumptions.

To build it, map each evidence signal to positive and negative deltas for each family. Then add contradiction penalties. Then expose a full trace: “pressure increased because untimed stable, timed weak.” This is where your engine starts behaving scientifically.

1.9 Intervention Memory Adjuster
This is the third layer of the current audited hypothesis system: working families are boosted, stalled families are penalized. 


Its role is to keep the engine from retrying the same explanation forever just because it was once plausible. If contrast repair failed three times, misconception should not keep ruling by inertia.

To build it, start with per-family attempt count and success rate. Then add recency weighting. Then add “used to work but now fading” detection. This module should be shared later with Adaptation, but Topic should own the local per-topic application of it.

1.10 Dominant Hypothesis Resolver
This sub-engine converts a multi-hypothesis ledger into one dominant cause plus optional co-causes.

Its role is to give the rest of the topic pipeline a usable working theory without pretending the theory is absolute truth. It must allow defer/no-dominant states when evidence is weak.

To build it, first use normalized scores plus margin thresholds. Then add evidence sufficiency gates. Then add special rules for keeping co-causes alive when two families are close. This should output dominant, co-causal, contradicted, stale, or defer.

1.11 Teaching Mode Selector
The audit confirms nine current modes with evidence override and reroute: PrerequisiteRollback, ContrastRepair, ConfidenceRebuild, FoundationBuild, ExamRescue, TransferTraining, VisualFirst, WorkedExampleFirst, ConceptCompression. 


Its role is to translate diagnosis into instructional posture. Diagnosis says what is wrong. Teaching mode says what kind of repair should happen next.

To build it, first preserve the existing family-to-mode mapping. Then implement evidence override rules. Then add learner-state compatibility checks so a mode is only eligible when the learner is ready for it.

1.12 Evidence Override Controller
This sub-engine is the “but actually” logic. Even if the dominant family suggests one mode, strong evidence may force another. The audit already notes that low transfer can force TransferTraining. 


Its role is to prevent brittle family-to-mode mapping. It is the engine’s safeguard against overly rigid diagnosis.

To build it, encode only a few strong overrides first: transfer, visual dominance, pressure, and retrieval compression. Then add a priority order so multiple overrides do not fight chaotically.

1.13 Intervention Reroute Controller
This sub-engine enforces the current reroute rule: when the primary hypothesis family is stalled at two or more attempts with under 50% effectiveness, shift to the next hypothesis’s recommended mode. 


Its role is to stop local stagnation.

To build it, begin with the exact audited rule. Then add dwell guards so the engine does not reroute too fast. Then add reroute audit logs because later Adaptation will learn from these pivots.

1.14 Mastery Proof Builder
The audit confirms the current six proof checkpoints: explain, independent solve, transfer, delayed recall, mixed context, light pressure. 


Its role is to define what counts as “done.” Without this sub-engine, the coach would confuse exposure with mastery.

To build it, first encode the current six dimensions. Then add optional dimensions such as justify method, discriminate neighbor, and recover after error. Then define required-by-topic-state rules so proof contracts scale with the depth of the topic.

1.15 Proof Evaluator
This sub-engine computes whether each checkpoint is met and whether advance_ready is true. The audit already states that all required checkpoints must be met and evidence capping prevents overclaiming. 


Its role is to turn proof from a static checklist into an evidence-backed gate.

To build it, combine profile-level averages with direct question evidence. Then add evidence floor requirements so a checkpoint cannot be called met with too little data. Then add cap logic tied to question-level support.

1.16 Delayed Recall Planner
The audit confirms delayed recall obligation is part of Topic and its scheduling is written into timing decisions. 


Its role is to make newly repaired topics prove themselves later, not just now.

To build it, start with simple fragile-vs-stable intervals. Then adapt intervals using learner forgetting speed. Then let Timing later consume the output and schedule it within the larger day plan.

1.17 Entry Point Resolver
The audit confirms Topic computes five entry-point types. 


Its role is to decide how the session starts: diagnose first, roll back, contrast repair, confidence recovery, or proof checkpoint. A good teaching mode with the wrong entry point can still fail.

To build it, map dominant hypothesis plus evidence sufficiency plus learner tension into a small entry-point enum. Then add tie-break rules for ambiguous cases.

1.18 Session Blueprint Builder
This sub-engine turns the topic decision into an actual teachable arc.

Its role is to generate the sequence of steps inside the session: explanation, contrast drill, worked example, independent solve, timed probe, reflection, and expected evidence. This is where the engine stops being diagnostic and becomes operational.

To build it, first create templates per teaching mode. Then add constraint logic: no too many heavy steps in a row, early win when fragile, contrast before independent discrimination for misconception cases. Then store the resulting blueprint as a reusable artifact.

1.19 Evidence Ingestion Engine
The audit confirms ingest_coach_episode_evidence() already normalizes coaching steps into evidence points with flags and hint-halving. 


Its role is to make sessions feed the brain. Without this, the engine cannot learn from what just happened.

To build it, define a canonical evidence point schema first. Then map every session step type into evidence points. Then add weighting, hint penalties, and deduping logic for repeated items.

1.20 Clinic Feedback Loop Closer
The audit confirms close_topic_clinic_feedback_loop() reruns topic analysis after every clinic session, infers outcome, writes intervention history, schedules delayed recall, and reloads intervention memory. 


Its role is to complete the hypothesis-test-verify cycle.

To build it, keep it as an orchestrator, not a scoring module. It should call ingestion, rerun analysis, classify the outcome, write history, schedule follow-up, and emit downstream events.

2. Coverage Intelligence
Parent role: portfolio-level analysis across subjects: are the right things covered, what is dangerously uncovered, and do we have enough sessions before the exam? The audit confirms its 7-bucket classification, 4 portfolio types, 8 gap types, Ebbinghaus review, time budget, and projected readiness. 


2.1 Topic Signal Loader
Its role is to pull Topic outputs such as proof contracts, evidence spines, and false-mastery signatures into the coverage layer. The audit explicitly says Coverage begins by loading Topic Intelligence signals from cached ic_topic_teaching rows. 


To build it, make it a strict adapter around ic_topic_teaching, not a recomputation module.

2.2 Bucket Classifier
This sub-engine assigns each topic to urgent_core, urgent_recoverable, high_yield_priority, mock_sensitive, exam_near_protection, fragile_maintenance, or low_yield_deferrable. Topic overrides can bump seemingly stable topics upward when proof is unmet. 


Its role is to convert a messy topic inventory into an action-shaped map.

To build it, implement deterministic bucket rules first. Then inject Topic override hooks. Then add audit explanations for each bucket assignment.

2.3 Portfolio Allocator
This sub-engine decides how much study time goes to rescue, improvement, protection, and maintenance. The audit already defines those four portfolio types and their time ranges. 


Its role is to prevent local topic urgency from hijacking the whole subject plan.

To build it, first derive portfolio percentages from bucket composition. Then add smoothing so allocations do not swing wildly between recomputes.

2.4 Coverage Gap Detector
The audit confirms eight gap types, including uncovered high yield, decaying gains, blocked downstream, false mastery suspect, proof blocked, speed gap, prerequisite chain, and over-invested. 


Its role is to explicitly name what kind of incompleteness exists.

To build it, make each gap type its own detector function. Then combine them into a CoverageGapMap. This should be a named-diagnostics module, not one giant conditional.

2.5 Review Obligation Planner
This sub-engine uses the Ebbinghaus-derived review interval model the audit describes. 


Its role is to distinguish new learning debt from review debt.

To build it, start with the current interval formula and overdue rules. Then add Topic-forced review hooks when delayed recall pressure exists.

2.6 Session Demand Estimator
The audit confirms a six-factor complexity-aware session estimation model with proof penalties. 


Its role is to estimate effort, not just need.

To build it, define a per-topic session demand formula. Then aggregate by subject. Then let Planner consume this later for schedule realism.

2.7 Time Budget Analyzer
Its role is to compare sessions available to sessions needed. The audit already computes available sessions from historical rate and flags triage when deficit exceeds five. 


To build it, keep it simple and transparent first. Then add uncertainty bands so projections are not falsely precise.

2.8 Coverage Trajectory Projector
The audit confirms it projects readiness by exam day and uses a 70% projected threshold for will_be_ready. 


Its role is to make coverage future-aware.

To build it, start with stable ratio, weekly improvement, and session budget. Then add scenario bands: pessimistic, likely, optimistic.

3. Sequencing Intelligence
Parent role: decide the order in which topics should be experienced. The audit confirms 7 models, 11 pipeline steps, historical penalties, interference separation, churn scoring, and pivot conditions. 


3.1 Signal Gatherer
Its role is to assemble the 16 sequencing signals the audit mentions. 


To build it, define a SequencingSignalSet first, then compute each signal independently so model competition stays clean.

3.2 Model Scorer
This sub-engine scores all seven models: DependencyFirst, BottleneckFirst, ConfidenceFirst, ExamWeightFirst, EasiestWinFirst, MisconceptionFirst, and Hybrid. 


Its role is to turn current learner state into candidate sequence strategies.

To build it, start with additive score bonuses per matching condition. Then add normalization and explanation strings.

3.3 Historical Penalty Engine
The audit confirms a penalty of 0.15 when a model was used three or more times in 14 days with under 30% success. 


Its role is to prevent repeating failed ordering logic.

To build it, read prior sequencing decisions and session outcomes, then apply penalties after raw model scoring.

3.4 Topic Ordering Engine
Its role is to generate the actual ordered list once a model wins.

To build it, create one ordering function per model. Keep ordering deterministic first. Later add tie-breaks from Risk and Coverage.

3.5 Alternative Comparison Builder
Its role is to store runner-up models and their reasons. This matters because later pivots should know what the likely next sequence would be.

To build it, persist the top three model scores and key reasons, not just the winner.

3.6 Pivot Condition Builder
The audit says Sequencing defines universal and model-specific pivot conditions. 


Its role is to tell the system when to stop trusting the current order.

To build it, start with static exit rules per model. Then add learner-state and exam-proximity modifiers.

3.7 Interference Separation Engine
The audit confirms confusion-score-based minimum gaps of 2, 3, or 4 positions. 


Its role is to stop damaging adjacency.

To build it, read contrast pairs, detect adjacency violations, then push the lower-priority topic backward until the minimum gap is satisfied.

3.8 Stability Monitor
The audit already computes churn from model churn, positional churn, and top-3 changes, with instability above 0.4. 


Its role is to keep the sequence from changing so often that the learner never settles.

To build it, compute churn after every sequencing decision and expose warnings to Adaptation and Planner.

4. Timing Intelligence
Parent role: govern when things happen. The audit confirms phase model, review scheduling, retests, golden windows, daily pacing, session frequency monitoring, and actionable timing decisions like review_topic, retest_topic, phase_transition, start_timed_work, run_mock, and stop_new_learning. 


4.1 Phase Resolver
Its role is to determine which of the seven macro phases the learner is currently in: map_and_stabilize through final_readiness. 


To build it, start with deterministic thresholds from readiness, at-risk count, stable ratio, and days to exam.

4.2 Phase Transition Evaluator
Its role is to decide whether the learner should move forward, stay, or step back.

To build it, separate entry conditions from exit conditions. Then add rollback rules when risk spikes or progress stalls.

4.3 Phase Health Monitor
The audit already includes stall detection based on expected durations plus a three-day grace period. 


Its role is to catch being stuck even when the phase technically still fits.

To build it, persist phase start timestamps and compare against expected windows.

4.4 Review Scheduler
Its role is to schedule recall-based reviews across topics.

To build it, use Coverage’s review obligations plus Topic’s delayed-recall obligations, then resolve clashes by urgency.

4.5 Retest Scheduler
The audit confirms three retest categories: fragile gains, post-intervention verification, and compaction checkpoints. 


Its role is to schedule proof rechecks, not just reviews.

To build it, create a retest queue table and tag each retest with reason, difficulty, and due date.

4.6 Golden Window Detector
The audit confirms 24h windows for fragile topics and 48h for stable ones, with reinforcement types based on recall and fragility. 


Its role is to exploit fresh gains before they leak away.

To build it, look for recent improvements lacking reinforcement. Then classify the reinforcement type.

4.7 Mode Readiness Controller
The audit says Timing computes six mode decisions. 


Its role is to govern whether the learner is ready for timed work, mocks, new learning, recovery, or stop-new-learning mode.

To build it, expose simple booleans first. Then turn them into richer readiness objects for Planner.

4.8 Daily Pacing Engine
The audit already includes intensity, rest detection, cooldown, and recommended session counts. 


Its role is to answer “what should happen today?”

To build it, combine current phase, exam distance, and recent session load. Then emit a compact day plan.

4.9 Session Frequency Monitor
Its role is to detect over-studying and under-studying. The audit already defines those thresholds. 


To build it, use rolling windows and compare actual to recommended frequency.

5. Risk Intelligence
Parent role: protect the learner and the plan. The audit confirms 12 risk classes, 8 compound patterns, 3 early warning types, risk velocity, risk-adjusted priorities, and three-scope persistence. 


5.1 Global Signal Calculator
Its role is to compute days to exam, time pressure, and global intervention failure before topic-level risk scoring begins. The audit lists this as step one. 


To build it, make it a pure precompute stage feeding all later risk assessment.

5.2 Topic Risk Assessor
Its role is to score each topic across the 12 current risk classes: false mastery, prerequisite, retention decay, confidence collapse, overload, time-to-exam, plan drift, surprise fragility, intervention failure, content quality, interference, and regression. 


To build it, implement one scorer per class. Then aggregate with weights.

5.3 Subject Risk Aggregator
Its role is to convert many topic risks into subject-level protection views.

To build it, aggregate averages plus top dangers plus dispersion. A subject with average moderate risk but one catastrophic topic should still look dangerous.

5.4 Compound Pattern Detector
The audit confirms eight compound patterns, including interference plus false mastery and false mastery plus time pressure. 


Its role is to detect nonlinear danger.

To build it, define explicit pattern rules with severities and explanations. This should not be a black box.

5.5 Protection Rule Generator
Its role is to convert risks into policy actions: protect, stop, isolate, compress, re-diagnose, or delay.

To build it, map compound and class-level severities to action templates. Then add expiry rules so protections are not permanent.

5.6 Early Warning Engine
The audit confirms three early warning types: recall_decay_approaching, fragile_new_learning, and interference_proximity. 


Its role is to warn before failure is visible in scores.

To build it, implement projection logic separately from current risk scoring.

5.7 Risk Priority Builder
The audit already computes risk-adjusted priorities as risk × importance × velocity boost, with 1.3x boost if rising. 


Its role is to decide what deserves urgent attention from the planner.

To build it, keep the formula simple and expose top-N outputs.

5.8 Trend Analyzer
The audit already tracks per-class deltas from seven days ago. 


Its role is to distinguish high-but-improving from medium-but-worsening risk.

To build it, compare historical class means and emit trend labels.

6. Adaptation Intelligence
Parent role: change strategy when evidence contradicts the current plan, while learning what works for this particular student. The audit confirms strategy health, intervention effectiveness, triggers, velocity/diminishing returns, cross-topic systemic patterns, learner adaptive profile, pacing guardrails, and stop lists. 


6.1 Strategy Health Analyzer
Its role is to summarize whether the current approach is improving, stalling, or regressing.

To build it, aggregate intervention outcomes, topic-case statuses, and recent trend direction into one health object.

6.2 Intervention Effectiveness Reporter
Its role is to compute per-family success rates and compare recent performance to older performance. The audit already does this. 


To build it, start with simple success rates. Then add diminishing-returns detection.

6.3 Trigger Evaluator
The audit currently has 10 listed triggers, even though the section header says 9. They cover stalled topic cases, confidence tension, plan tension, time pressure with moderate mastery, low recall across many topics, old weak strategies, failing families, velocity decline, and cross-topic systemic patterns. 


Its role is to decide whether adaptation should fire and in what mode: reopen diagnosis, reroute, protect, compress, or refine.

To build it, implement each trigger separately and let the evaluator merge them by urgency.

6.4 Velocity Analyzer
The audit already compares the last seven days with the prior seven and detects diminishing returns where a family used to work but now drops below 30%. 


Its role is to tell the coach whether momentum is improving or flattening.

To build it, define a stable rolling-window comparison function. Then expose both learner-level and family-level velocity.

6.5 Systemic Pattern Detector
The audit confirms four systemic patterns: family_systemic_failure, subject_regression_wave, systemic_speed_deficit, and systemic_recall_decay. 


Its role is to detect when the problem is no longer local.

To build it, implement pattern scanners over recent topic-case summaries. These should run after local topic analysis is already complete.

6.6 Learner Adaptive Profile Builder
The audit already says this profile learns family preferences, successful session length, pressure response, learns-from-mistakes behavior, confidence scaffolding need, and mode preferences by topic type. 


Its role is to make the coach personal rather than generic.

To build it, define a learner model schema first. Then fill one field at a time from reliable evidence. Do not overfit early.

6.7 Adaptation Pacing Guard
The audit already enforces dwell rules: over-adapting is 3+ adaptations in 3 days or 5+ in 7 days with dwell remaining, with 24h minimum dwell after major changes and 12h after minor ones. 


Its role is to stop strategy thrashing.

To build it, track adaptation timestamps and modes, then filter low-urgency recommendations during dwell.

6.8 Stop List Builder
The audit already defines four stop-list categories: ineffective families, stop new content when regression dominates, stop diminishing-return families, and stop over-reviewing stable topics while stalled topics exist. 


Its role is to make the engine say “no” as well as “do this.”

To build it, store negative recommendations explicitly so Planner can enforce them.

6.9 Recommendation Filter and Writer
Its role is to package the final adaptation decisions, reasoning, urgency, and strategy diffs into persistent form.

To build it, filter by dwell and urgency, then write compact decision rows that Planner can read directly.

7. How to get them built
Do not build all sub-engines at once. The audit already shows strong cross-engine dependencies: Topic writes teaching decisions, evidence, intervention history, and timing signals; Coverage reads Topic teaching rows; Timing reads intervention history and timing decisions; Risk reads topic cases and intervention history; Adaptation reads intervention history and topic cases; Planner reads Risk, Adaptation, and Timing. 


The correct build order is:

Phase 1: Formalize the existing parents
Wrap the six current engines in shared contracts:

common request/response envelope

common confidence bundle

common event types

common persistence ownership

This gives you stable parent engines without changing their logic yet.

Phase 2: Deepen Topic first
Build these sub-engines first:

Topic Scope Loader

Concept State Assembler

Evidence Spine Builder

Hypothesis Seeder

Hypothesis Evidence Adjuster

Teaching Mode Selector

Proof Evaluator

Session Blueprint Builder

Clinic Feedback Loop Closer

Topic should go first because it is already the deepest engine and feeds multiple others.

Phase 3: Add the control layer
Then build:

Coverage Gap Detector

Bucket Classifier

Model Scorer

Interference Separation Engine

Phase Resolver

Retest Scheduler

Topic Risk Assessor

Trigger Evaluator

Learner Adaptive Profile Builder

At this stage the system becomes strategically coherent.

Phase 4: Add memory and self-correction
Then build:

Historical Penalty Engine

Trend Analyzer

Velocity Analyzer

Systemic Pattern Detector

Adaptation Pacing Guard

Stop List Builder

This is where the coach stops being reactive and becomes self-correcting.

Phase 5: Add planner conflict handling
Once the sub-engines are stable, formalize conflict resolution between:

Risk protection

Timing readiness

Coverage debt

Sequencing order

Adaptation recommendations

Without this, deeper sub-engines will produce conflicting truths with no disciplined winner.

8. The simplest practical standard for every sub-engine
Every sub-engine should have the same implementation frame:

Belongs to: parent engine
Mission: one question it answers
Reads: exact inputs/tables/artifacts
Writes: exact outputs/tables/artifacts
Pure or stateful: whether it computes only or persists
Trigger: what wakes it up
Invariants: what it must never violate
Debug trace: why it decided what it decided
Tests: happy path, contradiction path, edge case, stale data case

If you enforce that format, the system will stay buildable even as it grows toward 100k+ lines.

The strongest next step is to turn this atlas into a sub-engine registry document with one page per sub-engine:

parent engine

purpose

inputs

outputs

algorithms

thresholds

persistence

events

failure modes

test cases


proceed

I’m converting the atlas into a stricter registry now: parent engine, mission, inputs, outputs, build order, persistence, and failure modes per sub-engine. I’ll start with Topic in full depth, then carry the same format across Coverage, Sequencing, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation.

Thought for 22s
Below is the Sub-Engine Registry Document v1.

It sits on top of the audited parent-engine layer you already have: Topic Intelligence as the deepest per-topic reasoning engine, Coverage for portfolio completeness and time budget, Sequencing for order-model selection and stability, Timing for phases/reviews/retests, Risk for 12-class protection and compound warnings, and Adaptation for trigger-based strategy revision and learner-specific learning memory. 

 

 


I am using one standard format for every sub-engine:

Belongs to

Core role

What it should read

What it should produce

Why it exists

How to build it

What “done” looks like

1. Topic Intelligence sub-engine registry
This parent engine already has the richest audited structure: an 18-step pipeline, 7 hypothesis families, 9 teaching modes with override/reroute, a 6-checkpoint proof contract, a 17-metric evidence spine, and clinic-loop closure.

1.1 Topic Scope Loader
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Define the bounded graph for the current topic. It decides what counts as the topic core, which prerequisite bridge concepts must be included, and which nearby confusion neighbors should be in scope.

What it should read:
j_topic_clusters, j_concept_edges, prerequisite links, contrast pairs, misconception edges, current topic ID.

What it should produce:
A TopicScope object with:

core concepts

prerequisite bridge concepts

interference neighbors

excluded concepts

graph depth metadata

Why it exists:
Without this, diagnosis becomes too wide and noisy or too narrow and blind. A topic engine should not reason over an entire subject graph every time.

How to build it:
Phase 1: deterministic graph extraction from cluster + bridge rules.
Phase 2: add radius control and edge-type filtering.
Phase 3: add “hot interference neighbor” inclusion for confusion-heavy pairs.
Phase 4: add trace output so you can inspect why each concept was included.

What “done” looks like:
Given any topic, the loader always returns the same bounded scope for the same graph state, and the team can inspect exactly why each concept was included.

1.2 Concept State Assembler
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Construct a state snapshot for each concept in the topic scope.

What it should read:
c_topic_profile, ic_compaction_state, concept metadata, misconception exposure, local evidence summaries.

What it should produce:
A list of ConceptState rows, each containing:

readiness

fragility

recall

transfer sensitivity

speed sensitivity

confidence sensitivity

abstraction level

misconception risk

proof depth

dependency weight

Why it exists:
The topic engine cannot reason directly over raw tables. It needs a normalized per-concept state layer first.

How to build it:
Start with a struct and fill only audited fields. Then add derived fields like dependency pressure and blocked-downstream count. Keep this module descriptive, not interpretive.

What “done” looks like:
A debugger can inspect one concept row and understand its current academic state without reading five other tables.

1.3 Concept Ranker
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Score concepts to identify which ones matter most right now.

What it should read:
ConceptState[]

What it should produce:
A ranked concept list with:

final rank score

per-dimension score contributions

compaction penalty

justification trace

Why it exists:
The topic engine needs a principled way to prioritize internal concept attention. Your audited engine already uses 8 dimensions plus compaction penalty; this sub-engine should preserve that as the formal concept-priority core. 


How to build it:
Implement the current weighted formula first. Then add per-dimension trace output. Then add sensitivity tests so small changes do not unpredictably reorder the top three.

What “done” looks like:
You can explain why concept A outranked concept B in one sentence using the trace.

1.4 Bottleneck Detector
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Identify the concept whose weakness is causing the largest downstream blockage.

What it should read:
Ranked concepts, dependency graph, downstream counts, recent error concentration.

What it should produce:
A BottleneckDiagnosis:

bottleneck concept ID

blockage severity

blocked descendants

confidence

alternative candidates

Why it exists:
The worst-looking concept is not always the most important one to repair first. This sub-engine identifies structural blockage, not just local weakness.

How to build it:
Version 1: count blocked descendants.
Version 2: weight descendants by exam leverage.
Version 3: blend downstream structure with observed local error density.

What “done” looks like:
The engine can distinguish “weak but isolated” from “weak and poisoning the topic.”

1.5 False Mastery Detector
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Catch topics that look fine on profile but are actually unstable.

What it should read:
Topic mastery state, transfer success, delayed recall success, mixed-context success, timed-vs-untimed gap.

What it should produce:
A FalseMasteryAssessment:

score

subtype

evidence reasons

flag on/off

Why it exists:
This prevents the coach from advancing too early. Your audited system already surfaces false mastery inside Topic and reuses it downstream in Coverage and Risk.

How to build it:
Start with hard rules. Then convert to a weighted score. Then split into subtypes:

recall false mastery

transfer false mastery

pressure false mastery

interference false mastery

What “done” looks like:
Stable-looking topics can still be correctly marked unsafe without hand-written exceptions elsewhere.

1.6 Evidence Spine Builder
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Aggregate question/session evidence into a topic evidence summary.

What it should read:
ic_topic_evidence_points, j_responses, j_question_instances, relevant coaching episode data.

What it should produce:
A TopicEvidenceSpine with the audited core metrics:

recent accuracy

weighted accuracy

hint dependency

avg response time

slow response rate

timed success

transfer success

mixed-context success

delayed-recall success

retrieval success

representation variety

recurring misconceptions
plus volume and recency metadata.

Why it exists:
Diagnosis should rely on evidence patterns, not just static profile values.

How to build it:
Phase 1: metric aggregators.
Phase 2: recency weighting.
Phase 3: contradiction detectors such as “strong untimed, weak timed.”
Phase 4: evidence sufficiency scoring.

What “done” looks like:
The spine becomes the authoritative evidence object used by hypotheses, proof, and feedback-loop outcome inference.

1.7 Hypothesis Seeder
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Generate the initial plausible causes for why the topic is not healthy.

What it should read:
Concept states, bottleneck diagnosis, false mastery assessment, tension context.

What it should produce:
A hypothesis ledger seeded across the audited seven families:

PrerequisiteWeakness

Misconception

TransferWeakness

RetrievalWeakness

PressurePerformance

KnowledgeDeficit

ConfidenceEmotional 


Why it exists:
The topic engine should begin with multiple plausible explanations, not one.

How to build it:
Start with deterministic rule templates per family. Then move to weighted seed scores. Then add multi-cause support.

What “done” looks like:
Every topic diagnosis begins with a ranked causal field, not a premature verdict.

1.8 Hypothesis Evidence Adjuster
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Update hypothesis confidence using the evidence spine.

What it should read:
Hypothesis ledger, evidence spine.

What it should produce:
Adjusted hypothesis scores and contradiction notes.

Why it exists:
The audit already confirms this second layer exists: hint dependency boosts prerequisite/knowledge, low transfer boosts transfer, delayed recall boosts retrieval, timed failure boosts pressure, recurring misconceptions boost misconception. This sub-engine formalizes that behavior.

How to build it:
Implement per-family evidence deltas. Then add negative deltas when evidence contradicts a family. Then log every change.

What “done” looks like:
For any hypothesis score, you can show exactly what evidence moved it up or down.

1.9 Intervention Memory Adjuster
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Update hypothesis confidence using past intervention outcomes.

What it should read:
ic_intervention_history, current hypothesis ledger.

What it should produce:
Memory-adjusted hypothesis scores, stall markers, reactivation markers.

Why it exists:
A cause that was plausible last week should not stay dominant if its repair family keeps failing. The audit confirms Topic already boosts working families and penalizes stalled ones.

How to build it:
Version 1: attempt count + success rate.
Version 2: recency weighting.
Version 3: diminishing-returns detection.

What “done” looks like:
The topic engine can stop retrying the same family out of habit.

1.10 Dominant Hypothesis Resolver
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Select one dominant cause plus optional co-causes, or defer if evidence is weak.

What it should read:
Seeded hypotheses, evidence-adjusted hypotheses, memory-adjusted hypotheses, evidence sufficiency.

What it should produce:
A HypothesisDecision:

dominant family

co-causes

dominance margin

confidence bundle

defer flag if needed

Why it exists:
The rest of the engine needs a working theory, but not a false certainty.

How to build it:
Use normalized scores plus margin thresholds. Then add no-dominant rules when evidence is insufficient.

What “done” looks like:
The engine can honestly say “we are not sure yet” instead of overcommitting.

1.11 Teaching Mode Selector
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Map the topic’s working causal theory into an instructional mode.

What it should read:
Dominant hypothesis, co-causes, learner state, evidence spine.

What it should produce:
One of the audited nine teaching modes.

Why it exists:
Diagnosis says what is wrong; mode selection says how the coach should respond.

How to build it:
Implement the direct family-to-mode map first. Then add readiness checks so unsafe modes cannot be selected for protected learners.

What “done” looks like:
Mode choice is explainable and auditable, not buried in many scattered conditions.

1.12 Evidence Override Controller
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Override the default teaching mode when strong evidence demands another posture.

What it should read:
Default mode, evidence spine, representation profile.

What it should produce:
Possibly replaced teaching mode plus override reason.

Why it exists:
The audit already confirms evidence override behavior, such as low transfer forcing TransferTraining. This prevents rigid diagnosis-to-mode mapping.

How to build it:
Only encode a few high-confidence overrides first:

transfer override

visual dominance override

retrieval compression override

pressure rescue override

What “done” looks like:
You can point to a single evidence rule and explain why the mode changed.

1.13 Intervention Reroute Controller
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Move away from the current family/mode when it has clearly stalled.

What it should read:
Primary family, intervention memory, runner-up hypotheses.

What it should produce:
Reroute decision, replacement mode, reroute reason.

Why it exists:
The audited rule is already explicit: reroute activates when the primary family is stalled at 2+ attempts and under 50% effectiveness.

How to build it:
Implement the audited rule exactly first. Then add dwell guards and anti-thrashing logic later.

What “done” looks like:
Repeatedly weak intervention families stop dominating future topic sessions.

1.14 Entry Point Resolver
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Choose how the session should begin.

What it should read:
Dominant hypothesis, evidence sufficiency, tension context, proof state.

What it should produce:
An entry-point enum such as:

DiagnoseFirst

RollbackPrerequisite

ContrastRepair

ConfidenceRecovery

ProofCheckpoint

Why it exists:
Even with the right teaching mode, the wrong opening move can damage the session.

How to build it:
Start with direct rules tied to dominant family and evidence confidence. Then add learner fragility modifiers.

What “done” looks like:
The start of each topic session is intentional, not accidental.

1.15 Mastery Proof Builder
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Define what mastery must mean for this topic and this state.

What it should read:
Topic state, dominant hypothesis, exam context.

What it should produce:
A ProofContract listing required checkpoints.

Why it exists:
The audit confirms the current six checkpoints and strict advance_ready logic. This sub-engine formalizes that contract before evaluation.

How to build it:
Encode the audited six first. Then add conditional dimensions like neighbor discrimination or recover-after-error.

What “done” looks like:
Every topic has an explicit mastery standard, not a vague notion of “good enough.”

1.16 Proof Evaluator
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Compute whether each proof checkpoint is met and whether advancement is allowed.

What it should read:
Proof contract, evidence spine, question-level proof evidence, topic profile.

What it should produce:
Checkpoint statuses, confidence per checkpoint, evidence cap, advance_ready.

Why it exists:
A contract without an evaluator is just documentation.

How to build it:
Combine profile and direct evidence. Add evidence floors. Add caps so weak evidence cannot falsely mark a checkpoint complete.

What “done” looks like:
The engine can prove why a topic is blocked from advancement.

1.17 Delayed Recall Planner
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Create delayed-recall obligations after meaningful learning events.

What it should read:
Topic improvement deltas, proof status, fragility, exam leverage, learner forgetting profile.

What it should produce:
Delayed recall tasks with due dates and purpose tags.

Why it exists:
The audit confirms Topic already checks delayed recall obligation and writes timing decisions.

How to build it:
Use simple intervals first, then learner-adjusted intervals later.

What “done” looks like:
Freshly improved topics reliably get verified later.

1.18 Session Blueprint Builder
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Generate the teachable session arc.

What it should read:
Teaching mode, entry point, proof contract, learner state, dominant hypothesis.

What it should produce:
A SessionBlueprint:

ordered steps

evidence expectations

stop conditions

fallback branch

estimated minutes

Why it exists:
This is the bridge from reasoning to real coaching action.

How to build it:
Start with mode templates. Then add constraints:

not too many heavy steps in a row

early win for fragile learners

contrast before independent discrimination for misconception cases

What “done” looks like:
Every topic decision produces an executable mini-plan.

1.19 Evidence Ingestion Engine
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Normalize what happened in a coaching episode into evidence points.

What it should read:
Completed coaching episode steps, hint usage, task flags, response outcomes.

What it should produce:
ic_topic_evidence_points rows with transfer/timed/mixed/delayed/retrieval flags and evidence weights. The audit already confirms this exists, including hint-halving.

Why it exists:
The engine must learn from each session in a standardized way.

How to build it:
Define canonical step-to-evidence mappings. Then add weighting and dedupe.

What “done” looks like:
Every meaningful coaching action becomes comparable evidence.

1.20 Clinic Feedback Loop Closer
Belongs to: Topic Intelligence

Core role:
Close the hypothesis-test-verify cycle after each clinic session.

What it should read:
Session outcome, newly ingested evidence, prior topic decision, intervention history.

What it should produce:
Updated topic decision, intervention outcome classification, delayed recall if needed, downstream events.

Why it exists:
The audit explicitly describes this as the hypothesis-test-verify cycle.

How to build it:
Keep it orchestration-only. It should call ingestion, rerun analysis, infer outcome, write intervention memory, schedule follow-up, then emit events.

What “done” looks like:
A finished session always changes system memory in a disciplined way.

2. Coverage Intelligence sub-engine registry
Coverage already loads Topic signals, runs 7-bucket classification with Topic overrides, builds 4 portfolio types, detects 8 gap types, computes review obligations, time budget, and readiness trajectory.

2.1 Topic Signal Loader
Belongs to: Coverage Intelligence

Core role:
Import cached Topic decisions into the coverage layer.

Reads:
ic_topic_teaching, proof contracts, false mastery signals, evidence summaries.

Produces:
A CoverageInputSnapshot per topic.

Why it exists:
Coverage should consume Topic intelligence, not recreate it.

How to build it:
Make it a thin adapter only.

Done when:
Coverage sees Topic’s proof, fragility, and false-mastery signals without recomputation.

2.2 Bucket Classifier
Belongs to: Coverage Intelligence

Core role:
Place topics into the audited seven buckets.

Reads:
Mastery state, readiness, fragility, recall, importance, exam proximity, Topic overrides.

Produces:
Per-topic bucket assignments.

Why it exists:
Coverage needs action-shaped grouping before it can allocate time.

How to build it:
Implement the raw classification rules first, then apply Topic override promotions.

Done when:
Two reviewers can inspect a topic and agree why it landed in its bucket.

2.3 Portfolio Allocator
Belongs to: Coverage Intelligence

Core role:
Allocate study effort across rescue, improvement, protection, and maintenance.

Reads:
Bucket distribution, average readiness, exam pressure.

Produces:
Subject-level allocation percentages.

Why it exists:
Without it, urgent topics can swallow all available time.

How to build it:
Start with the audited percentage bands. Then add smoothing.

Done when:
Portfolio percentages move gradually and stay explainable.

2.4 Coverage Gap Detector
Belongs to: Coverage Intelligence

Core role:
Detect the audited eight coverage gaps.

Reads:
Topic signals, graph information, session histories, recall state.

Produces:
A CoverageGapMap with named gaps.

Why it exists:
Named gaps are easier to act on than vague “coverage low.”

How to build it:
One detector per gap type. Then merge into one gap summary.

Done when:
Every coverage problem has a named diagnosis.

2.5 Review Obligation Planner
Belongs to: Coverage Intelligence

Core role:
Identify what review is overdue or approaching.

Reads:
Recall values, last review timestamps, delayed recall pressure, Ebbinghaus model inputs.

Produces:
Review obligations with urgency levels.

Why it exists:
Review debt is a distinct problem from new-learning debt.

How to build it:
Implement the audited interval model first. Then allow Topic-forced review overrides. 


Done when:
Coverage can distinguish “not yet learned” from “learned and decaying.”

2.6 Session Demand Estimator
Belongs to: Coverage Intelligence

Core role:
Estimate how many sessions each topic and subject still need.

Reads:
Readiness gap, fragility, transfer gap, speed gap, recall gap, importance, proof penalties.

Produces:
Per-topic and per-subject session demand estimates.

Why it exists:
Coverage should know effort, not just need.

How to build it:
Use the audited complexity-aware session estimation first. 


Done when:
The system can explain why one topic needs 2 sessions and another needs 7.

2.7 Time Budget Analyzer
Belongs to: Coverage Intelligence

Core role:
Compare sessions available to sessions needed before exam day.

Reads:
Historical session rate, days to exam, demand estimates.

Produces:
Budget balance, deficit or surplus, triage flag.

Why it exists:
Coverage must know when the plan is mathematically unrealistic.

How to build it:
Keep the audited formula first. Then add confidence bands. 


Done when:
The engine can say “you do not have enough runway” with evidence.

2.8 Coverage Trajectory Projector
Belongs to: Coverage Intelligence

Core role:
Project how covered the learner will be by exam day.

Reads:
Stable ratio, weekly improvement rate, session budget.

Produces:
Projected coverage and readiness confidence.

Why it exists:
Coverage should be future-aware, not just snapshot-aware.

How to build it:
Start with one likely scenario. Then add pessimistic and optimistic projections.

Done when:
The system can answer “will we likely be ready by then?”

3. Sequencing Intelligence sub-engine registry
Sequencing already chooses among seven models, applies historical penalties, defines pivot conditions, separates interference pairs, and monitors stability/churn. 


3.1 Signal Gatherer
Belongs to: Sequencing Intelligence

Core role:
Assemble all sequencing-relevant signals into one snapshot.

Reads:
Topic states, bottlenecks, confidence, exam weight, misconception pressure, learner state.

Produces:
SequencingSignalSet

Why it exists:
Model competition should not re-read raw tables directly.

How to build it:
Define the signal schema first. Then compute each signal independently.

Done when:
All sequence models score from the same clean input object.

3.2 Model Scorer
Belongs to: Sequencing Intelligence

Core role:
Score the audited seven models. 


Reads:
SequencingSignalSet

Produces:
Model scorecard with reasons.

Why it exists:
Order strategy should be an explicit decision, not hidden intuition.

How to build it:
Implement per-model scoring functions. Then normalize.

Done when:
The engine can explain why Hybrid beat BottleneckFirst.

3.3 Historical Penalty Engine
Belongs to: Sequencing Intelligence

Core role:
Penalize models that have recently underperformed.

Reads:
Sequence history, model outcome history.

Produces:
Adjusted model scores.

Why it exists:
The audit already shows historical penalties matter. 


How to build it:
Start with the audited penalty rule. Then add recency decay later.

Done when:
The engine stops repeating losing order strategies.

3.4 Topic Ordering Engine
Belongs to: Sequencing Intelligence

Core role:
Generate the ordered topic list after a model wins.

Reads:
Winning model, topic priorities, graph constraints.

Produces:
Ordered topic plan.

Why it exists:
Choosing a model is not the same as producing an order.

How to build it:
One deterministic ordering function per model.

Done when:
Any winner can produce a complete, reproducible order.

3.5 Alternative Comparison Builder
Belongs to: Sequencing Intelligence

Core role:
Store runner-up sequence models and tradeoffs.

Reads:
All model scores.

Produces:
Top alternatives with justifications.

Why it exists:
Useful for later pivots and debugging.

How to build it:
Persist top 3 model results, not just the winner.

Done when:
A pivot has a ready-made fallback candidate.

3.6 Pivot Condition Builder
Belongs to: Sequencing Intelligence

Core role:
Define when the sequence should be revisited.

Reads:
Model type, recent outcomes, churn, bottleneck clearance, confidence state.

Produces:
Pivot conditions and lock conditions.

Why it exists:
Without it, the order either changes too often or too late.

How to build it:
Hard-code per-model pivot rules first.

Done when:
The engine knows when to persist and when to reopen.

3.7 Interference Separation Engine
Belongs to: Sequencing Intelligence

Core role:
Ensure confusing topics are not scheduled too close together.

Reads:
Contrast pairs, confusion scores, proposed order.

Produces:
Adjusted order and anti-adjacency constraints.

Why it exists:
The audit confirms interference spacing is already a real function. 


How to build it:
Implement minimum-gap enforcement. Then add chain handling.

Done when:
Known confusion pairs stop appearing dangerously adjacent.

3.8 Stability Monitor
Belongs to: Sequencing Intelligence

Core role:
Measure sequence churn and instability.

Reads:
Current and previous sequence decisions.

Produces:
Churn score, lock recommendation, instability warnings.

Why it exists:
The learner needs a stable path, not constant reordering.

How to build it:
Compare model churn, positional churn, and top-3 changes.

Done when:
The system can detect “sequence thrashing.”

4. Timing Intelligence sub-engine registry
Timing already controls seven macro phases, review scheduling, retests, golden windows, daily intensity, session frequency, and mode readiness such as start_timed_work and run_mock. 


4.1 Phase Resolver
Belongs to: Timing Intelligence

Core role:
Determine the learner’s current macro phase.

Reads:
Stable ratio, at-risk ratio, mock readiness, exam proximity.

Produces:
Current phase.

Why it exists:
Timing decisions should be phase-aware.

How to build it:
Deterministic threshold rules first.

Done when:
Any learner snapshot maps cleanly to one phase.

4.2 Phase Transition Evaluator
Belongs to: Timing Intelligence

Core role:
Decide whether to stay, advance, or rollback phase.

Reads:
Current phase, phase health, recent progress, risk, exam proximity.

Produces:
Transition decision and reason.

Why it exists:
Phase should be dynamic, not static.

How to build it:
Separate entry, hold, and rollback conditions.

Done when:
The learner can move backward when timing realities worsen.

4.3 Phase Health Monitor
Belongs to: Timing Intelligence

Core role:
Detect stalled phases.

Reads:
Phase start date, expected phase duration, recent movement.

Produces:
Healthy/slipping/stalled.

Why it exists:
A phase can be technically correct but operationally stagnant.

How to build it:
Track elapsed days versus expected window plus grace.

Done when:
The engine can escalate when a phase lasts too long.

4.4 Review Scheduler
Belongs to: Timing Intelligence

Core role:
Schedule reviews across all due topics.

Reads:
Coverage review obligations, Topic delayed recall, learner daily capacity.

Produces:
Review queue.

Why it exists:
Someone has to decide when reviews actually land.

How to build it:
Merge obligations, then prioritize by fragility and exam leverage.

Done when:
Reviews stop being theoretical and become scheduled.

4.5 Retest Scheduler
Belongs to: Timing Intelligence

Core role:
Schedule topic retests and checkpoint re-verification.

Reads:
Fragile gains, intervention follow-up needs, compaction checkpoints.

Produces:
Retest queue.

Why it exists:
Retests are not the same as reviews.

How to build it:
Use the audited categories first. Then add difficulty policy. 


Done when:
Every retest has a reason and a due date.

4.6 Golden Window Detector
Belongs to: Timing Intelligence

Core role:
Detect near-term reinforcement opportunities after gains.

Reads:
Recent topic improvements, fragility, time since improvement.

Produces:
Golden-window actions.

Why it exists:
The audit already treats this as a special timing opportunity. 


How to build it:
Start with fragile vs stable windows.

Done when:
Fresh gains do not get left unreinforced.

4.7 Mode Readiness Controller
Belongs to: Timing Intelligence

Core role:
Control readiness for timed work, mocks, recovery mode, and stop-new-learning mode.

Reads:
Current phase, learner readiness, risk, recent overload.

Produces:
Mode-readiness decisions.

Why it exists:
Timing should decide not just when, but what intensity is safe.

How to build it:
Simple booleans first. Richer readiness states later.

Done when:
Planner can trust Timing for permissioning.

4.8 Daily Pacing Engine
Belongs to: Timing Intelligence

Core role:
Recommend daily intensity and session count.

Reads:
Current phase, overload, recent sessions, exam distance.

Produces:
Day plan intensity, rest/cooldown flags.

Why it exists:

How to build it:
Blend phase expectations with fatigue protection.

Done when:
The system can answer “how hard should today be?”

4.9 Session Frequency Monitor

Detect over-study and under-study patterns.

Reads:

Produces:
Frequency warnings.

Why it exists:

How to build it:
Use rolling windows.

Done when:
The engine can recognize pacing mismatch early.

5. Risk Intelligence sub-engine registry
Risk already computes 12 risk classes, 8 compound patterns, 3 early-warning types, velocity, class trends, and risk-adjusted priorities. 


5.1 Global Signal Calculator
Belongs to: Risk Intelligence

Core role:
Compute global background pressures before topic-by-topic scoring.

Reads:

Produces:
Global risk modifiers.

Why it exists:

How to build it:
Simple precompute module.

Done when:
All later risk scoring can reuse the same global context.

5.2 Topic Risk Assessor
Belongs to: Risk Intelligence

Core role:
Compute per-topic risk across the audited 12 classes. 


Reads:
Topic state, fragility, transfer, recall, downstream count, time pressure, intervention failure, content quality, interference.

Produces:
Per-topic risk row.

Why it exists:
Topic risk is the fundamental unit of protection.

How to build it:
One scorer per class. Then weighted aggregation.

Done when:
Each risk class remains inspectable.

5.3 Subject Risk Aggregator
Belongs to: Risk Intelligence

Core role:
Summarize topic risks at subject level.

Reads:
Topic risk rows.

Produces:
Subject risk summaries.

Why it exists:
Planner sometimes needs subject-level warnings, not just topic-level ones.

How to build it:
Average + max + concentration stats.

Done when:
One catastrophic topic cannot disappear inside a mild average.

5.4 Compound Pattern Detector
Belongs to: Risk Intelligence

Core role:
Detect the audited nonlinear compound risks. 


Reads:
Per-class risk rows.

Produces:
Compound risk alerts with severities.

Why it exists:
Some combinations are more dangerous than additive scoring shows.

How to build it:
Explicit pattern rules first.

Done when:
The engine can name “false mastery + time pressure” as a distinct disaster mode.

5.5 Protection Rule Generator
Belongs to: Risk Intelligence

Core role:
Translate risk into actions.

Reads:
Topic risks, compound risks, phase context.

Produces:
Protection actions such as:

protect

isolate

stop new learning

reduce intensity

re-diagnose

delay pressure

Why it exists:
Risk without policy is just alarm.

How to build it:
Map severity bands to action templates.

Done when:
Every major risk has a proposed protection.

5.6 Early Warning Engine
Belongs to: Risk Intelligence

Core role:
Predict problems before they fully materialize.

Reads:
Trend data, recall trajectories, newly taught fragile topics, confusion pairs.

Produces:
Early warnings.

Why it exists:
The audit already names recall decay, fragile new learning, and interference proximity as early-warning classes. 


How to build it:
Projection rules separate from current-state rules.

Done when:
The system warns before visible collapse.

5.7 Risk Priority Builder
Belongs to: Risk Intelligence

Core role:
Turn risk into prioritized planner input.

Reads:
Risk score, importance, velocity.

Produces:
Risk-adjusted priorities.

Why it exists:
The audit already uses risk * importance * velocity boost. 


How to build it:
Implement formula and keep top-N list.

Done when:
Planner receives a clean danger ranking.

5.8 Trend Analyzer
Belongs to: Risk Intelligence

Core role:
Measure which risks are improving or worsening over time.

Reads:
Current and historical risk rows.

Produces:
Per-class trend deltas.

Why it exists:
Medium risk that is rising may matter more than high risk that is falling.

How to build it:
Use seven-day comparison first, matching the audit. 


Done when:
Risk decisions become directional, not static.

6. Adaptation Intelligence sub-engine registry
Adaptation already monitors strategy health, family effectiveness, trigger conditions, velocity/diminishing returns, cross-topic systemic patterns, learner adaptive profile, dwell pacing, and stop lists.

Produces:
Strategy health state.

The system can say whether the current plan is still credible.

Belongs to: Adaptation Intelligence

Reads:
ic_intervention_history

Per-family effectiveness summaries.

How to build it:
Success rates first. Then recency and diminishing-returns layers.

Done when:
The engine knows not just what worked, but whether it is still working.

6.3 Trigger Evaluator

Detect when adaptation should fire.

Triggered adaptation proposals.

Encode each audited trigger separately, then merge.

You can inspect exactly which trigger caused a strategy change.

6.4 Velocity Analyzer

Measure whether progress is accelerating, flattening, or declining.

Velocity reports.

Windowed delta module first.

Done when:
The engine can see loss of momentum before collapse.

6.5 Systemic Pattern Detector

Detect cross-topic patterns that mean the problem is no longer local.

Reads:

Produces:
Systemic pattern alerts.

How to build it:
Pattern scanners over recent topic summaries.

Done when:
Adaptation can distinguish a topic issue from a system issue.

6.6 Learner Adaptive Profile Builder
Belongs to: Adaptation Intelligence

Core role:
Build the durable learner model.

Why it exists:
This is what makes the coach truly personal.

How to build it:
Add fields gradually. Only persist what has enough evidence.

Done when:
Other engines can query a stable learner-state fabric instead of guessing.

6.7 Adaptation Pacing Guard
Belongs to: Adaptation Intelligence

Core role:
Prevent strategy thrashing.

Reads:
Recent adaptation history, dwell timers, urgency.

Produces:
Over-adapting warnings and filtered recommendation sets.

Why it exists:
The audit already enforces dwell and over-adapting conditions. 


How to build it:
Implement the audited thresholds first.

Done when:
The coach cannot change strategy too often without strong reason.

6.8 Stop List Builder
Belongs to: Adaptation Intelligence

Core role:
Generate explicit “do not do this now” rules.

Reads:
Family effectiveness, strategy health, topic stability, regression patterns.

Produces:
Stop rules.

Why it exists:
Negative guidance is as important as positive guidance.

How to build it:
Start with the audited stop cases: ineffective families, stop-new-content during regression dominance, diminishing-return families, and over-reviewing stable topics while stalled topics exist. 


Done when:
Planner can obey “don’t keep doing this” just as clearly as “do this.”

6.9 Recommendation Filter and Writer
Belongs to: Adaptation Intelligence

Core role:
Package the final adaptation recommendations after dwell and urgency filtering.

Reads:
All upstream adaptation sub-engine outputs.

Produces:
Persistent adaptation decisions.

Why it exists:
The rest of the system needs one clean adaptation output, not raw internals.

How to build it:
Merge, filter, persist, and log reasons.

Done when:
Planner receives a compact, conflict-ready adaptation bundle.

7. How to get them built in the right order
Because your audited system already shows strong data flow between engines, the build order matters. Topic writes teaching decisions, evidence points, intervention history, and delayed recall scheduling; Coverage reads Topic teaching rows; Risk and Adaptation both rely on intervention history and topic-case quality; Planner already consumes Risk, Timing, and Adaptation outputs.

Stage 1: Stabilize the parent-engine contract
Build:

common engine request/response contract

confidence bundle

shared event taxonomy

persistence ownership boundaries

This gives all six parents a disciplined shell before deeper surgery.

Stage 2: Deepen Topic first
Build in this order:

Topic Scope Loader

Concept State Assembler

Evidence Spine Builder

Hypothesis Seeder

Hypothesis Evidence Adjuster

Intervention Memory Adjuster

Dominant Hypothesis Resolver

Teaching Mode Selector

Proof Builder

Proof Evaluator

Session Blueprint Builder

Evidence Ingestion Engine

Clinic Feedback Loop Closer

This is the right first family because Topic already has the richest logic and the heaviest downstream impact.

Stage 3: Build the strategic control layer
Then build:

Coverage Topic Signal Loader

Bucket Classifier

Coverage Gap Detector

Portfolio Allocator

Signal Gatherer

Model Scorer

Interference Separation Engine

Phase Resolver

Review Scheduler

Topic Risk Assessor

Strategy Health Analyzer

This makes the system strategically coherent across many topics.

Stage 4: Build the self-correcting memory layer
Then build:

Historical Penalty Engine

Trend Analyzer

Velocity Analyzer

Systemic Pattern Detector

Learner Adaptive Profile Builder

Adaptation Pacing Guard

Stop List Builder

This is when the coach stops behaving like a good static engine and starts behaving like a learning system.

Stage 5: Add planner conflict governance
Once the sub-engines are stable, add:

engine conflict registry

hard precedence rules

planner rationale tracing

deferred-action handling

That is where the whole intelligence system becomes operationally trustworthy.

8. The build standard every sub-engine should follow
Every sub-engine should live with this exact spec shape:

Name
Belongs to
Mission
Reads
Writes
Trigger
Pure or stateful
Invariants
Failure modes
Debug trace fields
Unit tests
Integration tests

If you enforce that structure, the system can grow very large without becoming vague.

1. Topic Scope Loader
Module path: topic_intelligence::concept_scope::topic_scope_loader

Role it plays:
This is the boundary setter. It decides what the engine is actually allowed to think about for one topic. It prevents the topic engine from reasoning over an entire subject or from missing prerequisite bridges that matter.

Belongs to main engine: Topic Intelligence

What it should read:

j_topic_clusters

j_concept_edges

prerequisite bridges

contrast pairs

misconception links

current topic metadata

What it should produce:
A TopicScope object:

core_concepts

prerequisite_bridges

interference_neighbors

excluded_neighbors

graph_depth

scope_reason_trace

Why it exists:
Your audited Topic engine is explicitly scoped to a bounded topic graph of cluster + prerequisite bridges, not a full subject. This sub-engine formalizes that promise. 


How to build it:
Start with a deterministic loader:

load cluster concepts for the topic

load 1-hop prerequisite bridges

load confusion neighbors from contrast/misconception links

dedupe and tag each node by inclusion reason

Then add controls:

max bridge depth

max interference neighbor count

include-by-score threshold

Then add debugging:

every included concept must carry an inclusion reason

Invariants:

core topic concepts must never be excluded

prerequisite bridges cannot exceed configured depth unless forced

interference neighbors must be tagged, not silently merged into core

Failure modes:

scope too wide, creating noisy diagnosis

scope too narrow, hiding bottlenecks

prerequisite loops

confusion neighbors flooding the graph

What done looks like:
For any topic, two reruns over unchanged data produce the same bounded scope and the trace clearly explains every included concept.

2. Concept State Assembler
Module path: topic_intelligence::concept_scope::concept_state_loader

Role it plays:
This turns raw concept/profile/proof/evidence fields into a normalized per-concept state row the rest of Topic Intelligence can reason over.

Belongs to main engine: Topic Intelligence

What it should read:

c_topic_profile

ic_compaction_state

concept metadata

misconception exposure

local evidence summaries 


What it should produce:
ConceptState rows such as:

readiness

fragility

recall

transfer_importance

speed_sensitivity

confidence_sensitivity

abstraction_level

misconception_risk

proof_depth

dependency_importance

exam_leverage

Why it exists:
The audited pipeline already gathers 16 fields per concept before ranking. This sub-engine should own that descriptive state assembly. 


How to build it:
Phase 1: define ConceptState and populate only audited fields.
Phase 2: add derived fields like dependency pressure and blocked descendants.
Phase 3: attach a field_sources trace so each value knows where it came from.

Invariants:

every concept in scope gets exactly one assembled state

state assembly is descriptive, not yet diagnostic

missing raw fields are explicit, never silently zeroed

Failure modes:

blending profile and evidence too early

stale compaction state

inconsistent normalization across fields

What done looks like:
You can inspect one concept row and understand its academic status without reopening source tables.

3. Concept Ranker
Module path: topic_intelligence::concept_scope::concept_ranker

Role it plays:
This chooses which concept matters most right now inside the topic.

Belongs to main engine: Topic Intelligence

What it should read:
ConceptState[]

What it should produce:
A ranked concept list with:

total rank score

per-dimension contributions

compaction penalty

final order

Why it exists:
The audited Topic engine already uses an 8-dimensional weighted rank plus compaction penalty. This is the first real prioritization layer in the pipeline. 


How to build it:
Build the current weighted ranker exactly first:

dependency importance

exam leverage

misconception risk

difficulty

transfer importance

speed sensitivity

confidence sensitivity

abstraction level

minus compaction penalty 


Then add:

trace output

stability tests

deterministic tie-break rules

Invariants:

all rank components are normalized before weighting

compaction penalty only subtracts, never inverts rank logic

tie-breaks are deterministic

Failure modes:

over-prioritizing high difficulty alone

underweighting blocked dependencies

unstable ordering on tiny score changes

What done looks like:
For any top-ranked concept, the engine can explain exactly why it won.

4. Bottleneck Detector
Module path: topic_intelligence::concept_scope::bottleneck_detector

Role it plays:
Find the concept whose weakness is structurally blocking the topic.

Belongs to main engine: Topic Intelligence

What it should read:

ranked concepts

dependency graph

blocked descendant counts

local error concentration

What it should produce:
BottleneckDiagnosis:

bottleneck concept

severity

blocked descendants

confidence

alternatives

Why it exists:
Rank priority and structural blockage are not the same thing. The topic may need to repair the poison source, not just the highest-score concept.

How to build it:
Version 1:

count downstream blocked nodes

Version 2:

weight blocked descendants by exam leverage

Version 3:

blend observed error concentration with graph structure

Invariants:

a bottleneck must have explainable downstream impact

low-confidence bottlenecks must expose alternatives

Failure modes:

mistaking isolated weakness for system blockage

graph cycles inflating blockage counts

What done looks like:
The engine can show why this concept is the one that must be unlocked first.

5. False Mastery Detector
Module path: topic_intelligence::concept_scope::false_mastery_detector

Role it plays:
Catch topics that look stable on surface metrics but are still unsafe.

Belongs to main engine: Topic Intelligence

What it should read:

topic mastery state

transfer success

delayed recall success

mixed-context success

timed/untimed gap

proof state

What it should produce:
FalseMasteryAssessment:

score

subtype

reasons

flag

Why it exists:
The audited Topic engine already detects false mastery and downstream engines use that signal to override normal classifications.

How to build it:
Phase 1:

deterministic rule: stable-looking topic + weak transfer/recall = suspect

Phase 2:

weighted false-mastery score

Phase 3:

subtype labels:

recall false mastery

transfer false mastery

pressure false mastery

interference false mastery

Invariants:

false mastery can flag stable-looking topics

flagging false mastery must not itself advance or regress the topic state

Failure modes:

over-flagging advanced topics

missing pressure-only false mastery

What done looks like:
The system can say “this looks learned, but it is not safe yet.”

6. Evidence Ingestion Engine
Module path: topic_intelligence::evidence::evidence_ingest

Role it plays:
Normalize coaching activity into reusable topic evidence points.

Belongs to main engine: Topic Intelligence

What it should read:

completed coaching steps

question outcomes

testing reason

hint usage

timed/transfer/mixed/delayed/retrieval flags

What it should produce:
ic_topic_evidence_points rows with weighted evidence. The audit confirms Topic already normalizes coaching steps into these rows and halves weight when hints are used. 


Why it exists:
Without it, the topic engine cannot learn from what just happened.

How to build it:
Define a canonical EvidencePoint schema first.
Then map each step type to:

base weight

evidence flags

misconception tags

optional representation type

Then add modifiers:

hint penalty

repeated-item penalty

diagnostic bonus

Invariants:

hints reduce evidence weight

all evidence points must point to topic + learner

evidence must be append-only

Failure modes:

duplicate evidence on replay

inconsistent weights

lost flags for timed or transfer steps

What done looks like:
Every meaningful step in a coaching episode becomes a comparable, queryable evidence point.

7. Evidence Spine Builder
Module path: topic_intelligence::evidence::evidence_spine

Role it plays:
Aggregate evidence into one topic-level truth object.

Belongs to main engine: Topic Intelligence

What it should read:

ic_topic_evidence_points

j_responses

j_question_instances

recent evidence windows

What it should produce:
A TopicEvidenceSpine covering the audited metrics:

total/recent/coach/journey points

recent/weighted accuracy

hint dependency

average response time

slow response rate

timed success

transfer success

mixed-context success

delayed-recall success

retrieval success

representation variety

recurring misconceptions 


Why it exists:
This is the evidence object that the rest of Topic Intelligence should trust.

How to build it:
Phase 1:

raw aggregations

Phase 2:

recency weighting

Phase 3:

contradiction pattern detectors like:

untimed strong, timed weak

direct solve OK, transfer weak

immediate success, delayed collapse

Phase 4:

evidence sufficiency score

Invariants:

spine must be reproducible from underlying evidence

contradiction patterns must not be flattened into one average

Failure modes:

averages hiding critical splits

recent evidence overwhelmed by stale data

What done looks like:
The topic engine can reason from a single evidence object without losing nuance.

8. Response Joiner
Module path: topic_intelligence::evidence::response_join

Role it plays:
Join evidence points with raw response metadata when deeper question-level interpretation is needed.

Belongs to main engine: Topic Intelligence

What it should read:

j_responses

j_question_instances

topic mappings

What it should produce:
Enriched response facts:

representation type

question family

misconception category

test context

timing context

Why it exists:
Evidence points are compressed. Sometimes diagnosis needs richer context from the raw response layer.

How to build it:

Invariants:

every joined row must preserve original IDs

enrichment must never mutate source data

Failure modes:

What done looks like:
