What it should read:

TopicRiskFeatures

GlobalRiskSignalSet

all 12 class scorers

What it should produce:
A TopicRiskAssessment:

12 class scores

weighted overall risk

top risk drivers

confidence

trace

Why it exists:
The audit’s second major step is: assess every topic across all 12 classes. 


How to build it:
Implement weighted aggregation using the audited class weights first.
Then add top-driver extraction and confidence scoring.

Invariants:

every class score must remain visible

overall risk must be reproducible from class scores

Failure modes:

black-box aggregation

overall score hiding dominant classes

What done looks like:
Each topic ends up with a full, inspectable risk row.

16. Subject Risk Aggregator
Module path: risk_intelligence::aggregate::subject_risk_aggregator

Roll topic-level risks into subject-level risk summaries.

What it should produce:
A SubjectRiskSummary:

average risk

top class drivers

metadata

Why it exists:
The audit’s third step is: aggregate risk by subject. 


How to build it:
Start with average + max + severe-count metrics.
Then add weighted importance summaries.

Invariants:

one catastrophic topic must not disappear inside a mild average

subject summary must expose concentration, not just mean

Failure modes:

average-only aggregation

What done looks like:
The planner can see whether a whole subject is broadly risky or narrowly dangerous.

17. Compound Pattern Detector
Module path: risk_intelligence::compound::pattern_detector

Role it plays:
Detect nonlinear combinations of risks that are more dangerous than individual class scores alone.

Belongs to main engine: Risk Intelligence

What it should read:

topic risk rows

class scores

sequencing/timing context where relevant

What it should produce:
Compound pattern alerts.

Why it exists:
The audit explicitly defines 8 compound risk patterns with severities, including:

interference + false_mastery

false_mastery + time_pressure

prerequisite_block + plan_drift

confidence_collapse + retention_decay

regression + time_pressure

overload + time_pressure

speed_deficit + time_pressure

retention_decay + overload 


How to build it:
Implement the audited 8 patterns exactly first.
Then add pattern trace and confidence.
Later add pattern-specific protection recommendations.

Invariants:

compound patterns must be explicitly named

severity must reflect both presence and strength of underlying classes

Failure modes:

patterns not surfacing because class scores are viewed in isolation

every co-occurrence treated as a compound risk

What done looks like:
The engine can say “this is not just two risks, this is a failure mode.”

18. Protection Rule Generator
Module path: risk_intelligence::protection::rule_generator

Role it plays:
Turn risks into protective actions that can override ordinary planning.

Belongs to main engine: Risk Intelligence

What it should read:

topic risk rows

subject risk summaries

compound pattern alerts

timing/phase context

What it should produce:
Protection actions such as:

protect topic

isolate confusion pair

reduce intensity

stop new learning

force review

reopen diagnosis

block timed work

Why it exists:
The audited pipeline’s fifth step is to generate protection rules. 


How to build it:
Start with direct mappings from:

severe class scores

severe compounds

rising-fast overall risk

Then add expiries and review conditions.

Invariants:

every protection rule must include reason and scope

protection rules should be reversible, not permanent

Failure modes:

risk scores with no actionable output

permanent protection states

too many blanket protections

What done looks like:
The engine can convert warning into usable policy.

19. Early Warning Engine
Module path: risk_intelligence::warnings::early_warning_engine

Role it plays:
Predict problems before they become full risk spikes.

Belongs to main engine: Risk Intelligence

What it should read:

stable topics

recall trajectories

recently taught topics

fragility

confusion pairs

recent study order

What it should produce:
Early-warning alerts.

Why it exists:
The audit defines 3 early warning types:

recall_decay_approaching

fragile_new_learning

interference_proximity 


How to build it:
Implement each warning separately:

recall decay approaching: stable topics with recall 0.4–0.6, projected critical in <14 days

fragile new learning: recently taught with fragility >0.6

interference proximity: confusion pair where one side was just studied

Invariants:

early warnings must be predictive, not just descriptive

warning must include what threshold was crossed

Failure modes:

warnings too late to be useful

warnings so broad they become noise

What done looks like:
The engine sees trouble while there is still time to prevent it.

20. Risk Velocity Analyzer
Module path: risk_intelligence::trends::velocity_analyzer

Role it plays:
Measure how fast overall risk is rising or falling.

Belongs to main engine: Risk Intelligence

What it should read:

current overall risk

last persisted overall risk

What it should produce:
A velocity score and status label.

Why it exists:
The audit explicitly defines Risk Velocity as current_overall - previous_overall, with >0.15 flagged as “RISK RISING FAST.” 


How to build it:
Implement the audited delta exactly first.
Then later add smoothing across multiple historical points.

Invariants:

velocity must be about change, not absolute level

previous comparison point must be explicit

Failure modes:

interpreting high risk as rising risk

noisy one-off jumps dominating everything

What done looks like:
The engine can distinguish stable-dangerous from rapidly-worsening.

21. Risk-Adjusted Priority Builder
Module path: risk_intelligence::priorities::priority_builder

Role it plays:
Convert risk into planner-ready urgency.

Belongs to main engine: Risk Intelligence

What it should read:

topic overall risk

topic importance

velocity status

What it should produce:
A ranked priority list.

Why it exists:
The audit defines Risk-Adjusted Priorities as risk * importance * velocity_boost, with a 1.3x boost if rising, and returns the top 15. 


How to build it:
Implement the audited formula exactly first.
Then add tie-breaks using severe-class count.

Invariants:

rising risk must materially increase planner visibility

priorities must expose component scores

Failure modes:

importance dominating actual danger

velocity ignored

top list unstable without tie-breaks

What done looks like:
The planner gets a compact list of what is both dangerous and important.

22. Per-Class Historical Trend Analyzer
Module path: risk_intelligence::trends::class_trend_analyzer

current class scores

ic_risk_assessments from 7 days ago

What it should produce:
Per-class deltas with labels:

improving

worsening

flat

Why it exists:
The audit explicitly defines this logic: query 7-day-ago per-class averages, compute true delta per class, improving if < -0.08, worsening if > 0.08. 


How to build it:
Use the audited 7-day comparison first.
Then later add rolling-window trend confidence.

Invariants:

trend must compare same class to same class

direction labels must be threshold-driven

Failure modes:

absolute level confused with trend

missing historical baselines

What done looks like:
The engine can say which specific dangers are growing or shrinking.

24. Three-Scope Persistence Writer
Module path: risk_intelligence::persist::assessment_writer

Role it plays:
Persist risk assessments at topic, subject, and phase scope.

Belongs to main engine: Risk Intelligence

What it should read:
All upstream Risk outputs.

What it should produce:
ic_risk_assessments rows with:

topic scope

subject scope

phase scope

per-class values

metadata

timestamps

Why it exists:
The audit explicitly defines 3-scope persistence: topic-level with 12 columns, subject-level with average risk plus metadata, and phase-level overall snapshot. 


How to build it:
Persist scope-specific row shapes under one canonical write interface.
Use upsert semantics first, matching the audit.
Then add decision-id linkage later.

Invariants:

topic, subject, and phase scope must remain distinguishable

writes must be idempotent for a recompute cycle

Failure modes:

topic and subject rows mixed together

stale scope rows not replaced

partial writes across scopes

What done looks like:
All risk state is queryable, historical, and planner-readable at the correct scope.

25. Build order for the Risk family
Risk sits downstream of Topic, Coverage, Sequencing, and Timing because it needs topic truth, intervention failure, confusion structure, time pressure, and plan state to assess danger. The audit confirms it consumes topic profile, graph data, academic events, topic cases, intervention history, concept coverage, contrast pairs, coaching episodes, previous risk rows, and subject blueprints. 


Recommended build sequence
Global Signal Calculator

Topic Risk Feature Assembler

False Mastery Risk Scorer

Prerequisite Risk Scorer

Retention Decay Risk Scorer

Confidence Collapse Risk Scorer

Overload Risk Scorer

Time-to-Exam Risk Scorer

Plan Drift Risk Scorer

Surprise Fragility Risk Scorer

Intervention Failure Risk Scorer

Content Quality Risk Scorer

Interference Risk Scorer

Regression Risk Scorer

Topic Risk Assessor

Subject Risk Aggregator

Compound Pattern Detector

Protection Rule Generator

Early Warning Engine

Risk Velocity Analyzer

Risk-Adjusted Priority Builder

Per-Class Historical Trend Analyzer

Risk Summary Builder

Three-Scope Persistence Writer

Why this order
It follows the audited risk flow directly:

compute global signals

score all topics across 12 classes

aggregate to subject scope

detect compounds

generate protections

detect early warnings

compute priorities

compute trends

summarize

persist at topic/subject/phase scope 


What the whole Risk family should look like when finished
By the end, Risk Intelligence should behave like a real protective nervous system:

it sees hidden danger even when profile scores look healthy

it distinguishes memory leak, time pressure, confidence collapse, intervention failure, interference, and regression instead of flattening them

it detects compound failure modes, not just isolated risks

it raises warnings before collapse becomes obvious

it knows whether risk is merely high or actively worsening

it feeds the planner a danger-weighted priority list

it can generate concrete protection rules instead of only alarms

it persists risk truth at topic, subject, and phase scope so later engines can reason from the same danger map

Next should be the Adaptation Intelligence Registry Pack.


proceed

I’m finishing the registry series with Adaptation now, keeping it pinned to the audited engine so it stays faithful to what already exists while expanding it into build-grade sub-engines.

Thought for 6s
Below is the Adaptation Intelligence Registry Pack.

This pack is grounded in your audited Adaptation engine: 1,337 lines, an 11-step pipeline, 10 listed adaptation triggers, velocity analysis with diminishing returns, 4 cross-topic systemic patterns, a learner adaptive profile, adaptation pacing with dwell times, and a 4-part stop list. It reads ic_topic_cases, ic_tensions, c_topic_profile, ic_intervention_history, ic_mission_state, c_coaching_episodes, j_concept_nodes, and ic_adaptation_log, and writes ic_adaptation_log, which the planner already uses for protect/compress/reroute signals. 

 

 


Adaptation Intelligence Registry Pack
Parent engine
Main engine: Adaptation Intelligence
Parent mission: change strategy when the evidence says the current plan is no longer the right one, while building a persistent model of what works for this specific learner.

Parent-owned outputs
Adaptation should remain the owner of:

1. Strategy Health Analyzer
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::strategy::health_analyzer

Role it plays:
Summarize whether the current overall strategy is healthy, slipping, stalled, or regressing.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

ic_topic_cases

recent topic outcomes

intervention outcomes

improvement/stalled/regressing counts

mission state

What it should produce:
A StrategyHealthReport:

overall effectiveness

improving count

stalled count

regressing count

health status

health confidence

Why it exists:
The audited pipeline starts by computing strategy health from effectiveness plus improving/stalled/regressing counts.

How to build it:
Version 1: aggregate counts and a simple weighted health score.
Version 2: separate topic-level from subject-level health.
Version 3: add decay so very old wins matter less.

Invariants:

strategy health must be based on recent evidence, not old reputation

improving/stalled/regressing counts must stay visible

Failure modes:

overall health hiding concentrated failure pockets

stale wins masking recent regression

What done looks like:
The engine can answer, in one object, whether the current strategy is still credible.

2. Intervention Effectiveness Reporter
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::interventions::effectiveness_reporter

Role it plays:
Measure which intervention families are working, fading, or failing.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

ic_intervention_history

intervention family

outcome classes

recency windows

What it should produce:
An InterventionEffectivenessReport:

family-by-family success rate

attempts

recent effectiveness

historical effectiveness

preferred families

avoided families

Why it exists:
The audited pipeline explicitly computes a per-family intervention effectiveness report, and the learner profile later uses preferred/avoided families from it.

How to build it:
Start with raw success rate by family.
Then add recency weighting.
Then add contrast between recent and historical performance.

Invariants:

family stats must expose sample size

recent and historical rates must stay distinct

Failure modes:

one lucky success over-inflating a family

recent collapse hidden inside a good all-time average

What done looks like:
The engine knows not just what works, but what is still working.

3. Trigger Evaluator
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::triggers::trigger_evaluator

Role it plays:
Check whether an adaptation should fire and what mode it should use.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

strategy health

tensions

time pressure

mastery state

recall density

family effectiveness

velocity status

systemic patterns

What it should produce:
A list of AdaptationTriggerHit rows:

trigger id

trigger reason

recommended mode

urgency

confidence

Why it exists:
The audited engine explicitly checks the adaptation triggers before strategy change. It lists triggers such as stalled topic cases, high confidence tension, high plan tension, time pressure with reasonable mastery, many low-recall topics, old weak strategies, failing families, declining velocity, and cross-topic systemic patterns.

How to build it:
Implement each trigger as a separate function first.
Then merge them by urgency and confidence.
Then add deduping when multiple triggers imply the same mode.

Invariants:

every adaptation recommendation must point to one or more trigger hits

triggers must stay independently testable

Failure modes:

monolithic trigger logic that is impossible to audit

noisy triggers firing constantly

What done looks like:
The engine can show exactly what evidence caused an adaptation proposal.

4. Trigger-to-Mode Mapper
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::triggers::mode_mapper

Role it plays:
Translate trigger hits into concrete adaptation modes.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

trigger hits

current strategy state

learner readiness

What it should produce:
Mode proposals such as:

protect

compress

reroute

reopen_diagnosis

refine

sequence_shift

timing_shift

content_shift

Why it exists:
The audited triggers already map to modes like protect, compress, reroute, reopen_diagnosis, and refine. 


How to build it:
Keep the direct audited trigger→mode mapping first.
Then add collision rules when several triggers propose different modes.

Invariants:

every mode proposal must preserve the originating trigger

stronger protection modes should dominate weaker refinements when needed

Failure modes:

too many conflicting modes

mode inflation from weak triggers

What done looks like:
The engine can move cleanly from “what went wrong” to “what kind of change is needed.”

5. Velocity Analyzer
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::velocity::velocity_analyzer

Role it plays:
Measure whether progress per unit effort is accelerating, flattening, or declining.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

last 7 days outcomes

previous 7 days outcomes

improvement per session

family-level recent rates

What it should produce:
A VelocityReport:

recent improvement rate

prior improvement rate

delta

declining/not declining flag

Why it exists:
The audited engine explicitly compares the last 7 days to the previous 7 days and uses this in adaptation. 


How to build it:
Start with simple windowed improvement/session comparison.
Then split by family and by subject later.

Invariants:

velocity must reflect change, not absolute performance

comparison windows must be explicit

Failure modes:

high performance misread as positive velocity

short noisy windows dominating

What done looks like:
The engine can tell whether the learner is still gaining momentum or losing it.

6. Diminishing Returns Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::velocity::diminishing_returns

Role it plays:
Detect when an approach used to work but is now losing effectiveness.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

historical family rate

recent family rate

intervention attempts by family

What it should produce:
A DiminishingReturnsAlert:

family

old rate

recent rate

severity

stop/refine recommendation

Why it exists:
The audited engine explicitly defines diminishing returns as families where old_rate > 50% but recent_rate < 30%, and notes that this is different from “it never worked.”

How to build it:
Implement the audited threshold exactly first.
Then later add slope-based severity.

Invariants:

diminishing returns requires historical success first

recent collapse must be visible as distinct from chronic failure

Failure modes:

all poor families mislabeled as diminishing returns

fading effectiveness ignored until total failure

What done looks like:
The engine can say “this family is wearing out,” not just “this family is bad.”

7. Systemic Pattern Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::systemic::pattern_detector

Role it plays:
Detect cross-topic patterns that suggest the issue is no longer local.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

topic cases across subjects

family-level failures

speed states

recall states

regression clusters

What it should produce:
Systemic pattern alerts.

Why it exists:
The audited engine explicitly defines four cross-topic systemic patterns:

family_systemic_failure

subject_regression_wave

systemic_speed_deficit

systemic_recall_decay

How to build it:
Implement each pattern as an independent detector first.
Then aggregate into one systemic alert set.

Invariants:

every systemic pattern must meet its own count threshold

local topic failure should not masquerade as systemic failure

Failure modes:

cross-topic patterns never surfacing

too many low-confidence systemic alarms

What done looks like:
The engine can distinguish “one topic is bad” from “our whole approach is wrong.”

8. Family Systemic Failure Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::systemic::family_systemic_failure

Role it plays:
Detect when the same intervention family is failing across many topics.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

family effectiveness by topic

intervention history

What it should produce:
A systemic failure alert for that family.

Why it exists:
The audited rule is explicit: same family failing across 3+ topics means the approach may be wrong for this student.

How to build it:
Count distinct failing-topic instances per family.
Then attach severity by failure depth.

Invariants:

must be distinct topics, not repeated failures in one topic

low sample sizes must not trigger this

Failure modes:

one bad topic poisoning a whole family

truly bad families escaping detection

What done looks like:
The engine can stop recommending a family that is broadly wrong for the learner.

9. Subject Regression Wave Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::systemic::subject_regression_wave

Role it plays:
Detect when several topics are regressing in the same subject at once.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

topic cases

recent regression markers

subject ids

What it should produce:
A regression-wave alert by subject.

Why it exists:
The audited rule is explicit: 3+ topics regressing in the same subject suggests a deeper foundational gap.

How to build it:
Count regressing topics per subject over a rolling window.
Then rank waves by severity and importance.

Invariants:

regression wave requires multiple topics in one subject

regressions must be recent

Failure modes:

stale regressions counted forever

broad weakness mislabeled as active wave without recency

What done looks like:
The engine can recognize when a subject is slipping as a whole.

10. Systemic Speed Deficit Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::systemic::speed_deficit

Role it plays:
Detect an exam-readiness issue caused by speed weakness across otherwise stable topics.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

topic stability

speed scores

recent timed outcomes

What it should produce:
A systemic speed deficit alert.

Why it exists:
The audited pattern is explicit: 4+ stable topics with speed < 0.3 indicates an exam-readiness gap.

How to build it:
Filter to stable topics first.
Then count low-speed topics.
Then score severity by count and exam proximity.

Invariants:

only stable topics count

low speed alone without stability should not trigger this systemic pattern

Failure modes:

knowledge weakness mislabeled as speed deficit

stable-but-slow pattern missed

What done looks like:
The engine can say “this learner understands, but exam pace is the systemic issue.”

11. Systemic Recall Decay Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::systemic::recall_decay

Role it plays:
Detect when too many okay-looking topics are quietly decaying.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

readiness

recall

review history

What it should produce:
A systemic recall decay alert.

Why it exists:
The audited rule is explicit: 5+ topics with OK readiness but recall < 0.4 indicates insufficient review ratio.

How to build it:
Filter to OK-readiness topics first.
Then count low-recall cases.
Then tie severity to how far recall has fallen.

Invariants:

readiness must be acceptable before this pattern applies

this is a review-ratio issue, not generic weakness

Failure modes:

low-readiness topics polluting the pattern

quiet recall leakage never being elevated systemically

What done looks like:
The engine can recommend a review-policy shift, not just local patching.

12. Learner Adaptive Profile Builder
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::learner_profile::profile_builder

Failure modes:

overfitting on tiny samples

global assumptions masquerading as learner truths

What done looks like:
Other engines can query a stable learner model instead of guessing preferences.

13. Session Duration Analyzer
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::learner_profile::session_duration

Role it plays:
Find the session length that tends to produce success for this learner.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

successful coaching episodes

duration

outcomes

What it should produce:
An average successful session duration plus confidence.

Why it exists:
The audited learner profile explicitly uses the average of successful sessions.

How to build it:
Start with mean duration across successful episodes.
Then add robust statistics later to reduce outlier distortion.

Invariants:

only successful sessions count

sample size must be exposed

Failure modes:

one long session skewing the average

failed marathon sessions inflating recommended duration

What done looks like:
The system can personalize session length from evidence.

14. Pressure Response Analyzer
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::learner_profile::pressure_response

Role it plays:
Measure how this learner behaves under timed conditions.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

timed-condition outcomes

accuracy under pressure

sample count

What it should produce:
A PressureResponseProfile.

Why it exists:
The audited learner profile uses success rate under timed conditions and requires 3+ data points.

How to build it:
Implement the 3+ datapoint minimum first.
Then classify into pressure-tolerant, pressure-fragile, or insufficient data.

Invariants:

no pressure profile with fewer than 3 valid timed datapoints

timed data must stay separate from untimed

Failure modes:

pressure profile inferred from too little data

untimed success leaking into pressure response

What done looks like:
The system can say whether this learner is genuinely okay under time pressure.

15. Learns-From-Mistakes Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::learner_profile::mistake_learning

Role it plays:
Detect whether the learner tends to improve after earlier failure on the same topic.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

repeated topic outcomes

prior failure markers

later improvement markers

What it should produce:
A LearnsFromMistakesSignal.

Why it exists:
The audited learner profile explicitly includes improved-after-previous-failure detection on the same topic.

How to build it:
Track repeated topic attempts over time.
Then compute whether failure→later-improvement patterns are common.

Invariants:

later success must follow earlier failure on the same topic

one-off recoveries should not overstate the signal

Failure modes:

improvement from random chance misread as pattern

genuine resilient learners not being identified

What done looks like:
The engine can personalize whether struggle tends to be productive for this student.

16. Confidence Scaffolding Need Estimator
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::learner_profile::confidence_scaffolding

Role it plays:
Measure how much confidence support the learner seems to need.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

confidence tension

collapse patterns

recovery speed

What it should produce:
A ConfidenceSupportNeed value.

Why it exists:
The audited learner profile explicitly derives confidence scaffolding need from confidence tension.

How to build it:
Use tension first.
Then add collapse/recovery modifiers.

Invariants:

support need should not be inferred from generic weakness alone

tension evidence must remain visible

Failure modes:

all weak learners labeled high-support

actual confidence-fragile learners not elevated enough

What done looks like:
The system knows whether to lead with protection and confidence support.

17. Teaching Mode Preference Builder
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::learner_profile::mode_preferences

Role it plays:
Learn which teaching modes work best for this learner by topic type.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

coaching episodes

topic type tags (abstract, procedural, factual, general)

mode used

outcomes

What it should produce:
Mode-preference maps by topic type and episode type.

Why it exists:
The audited profile explicitly includes teaching mode preferences by topic type.

How to build it:
Start with topic-type buckets and mean success rate per mode.
Then add confidence and minimum sample-size gates.

Invariants:

preferences must be conditional on topic type

low-sample preferences must stay tentative

Failure modes:

one lucky success defining a preference

no distinction between procedural and abstract learning

What done looks like:
The engine can personalize mode choice by content type.

18. Adaptation Pacing Guard
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::pacing::pacing_guard

Role it plays:
Stop the system from over-adapting and thrashing the learner.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

ic_adaptation_log

adaptation timestamps

current dwell state

trigger urgency

What it should produce:
An AdaptationPacingReport:

over-adapting or not

dwell remaining

allowed urgency floor

Why it exists:
The audited engine explicitly defines over-adapting as 3+ in 3 days OR 5+ in 7 days with dwell remaining, with minimum dwell of 24h after reopen_diagnosis/compress and 12h after minor changes. When over-adapting, it filters to urgency > 0.75 only.

How to build it:
Implement the audited thresholds exactly first.
Then add richer urgency windows later.

Invariants:

dwell must be time-based and explicit

over-adapting must change recommendation filtering behavior

Failure modes:

constant strategy thrash

urgent changes wrongly blocked by dwell

What done looks like:
The system can stay adaptive without becoming unstable.

19. Stop List Builder
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::stops::stop_list_builder

Role it plays:
Build explicit negative recommendations — what the system should stop doing.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

intervention effectiveness report

velocity report

strategy health

review allocation patterns

stalled topic density

What it should produce:
A StopList with categories.

Why it exists:
The audited engine explicitly defines four stop-list categories:

stop ineffective families

stop new content when regression > improvement

stop diminishing-return families

stop over-reviewing stable topics when stalled topics exist

How to build it:
Implement each stop class independently.
Then merge into one list with scope and rationale.

Invariants:

every stop rule must name what exactly should stop

stop rules must be evidence-backed

Failure modes:

vague negative recommendations

stop rules that cannot be enforced by Planner

What done looks like:
The engine can say “do less of this specific thing” with evidence.

20. Ineffective Family Stop Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::stops::ineffective_families

Role it plays:
Stop intervention families that are consistently failing.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

family effectiveness

family usage count

What it should produce:
Stop-family rules.

Why it exists:
The audited stop list says: stop ineffective families at 3+ uses and <25% success.

How to build it:
Implement the audited threshold exactly first.
Then add decay so families can be reconsidered later after enough time.

Invariants:

stop requires both enough uses and low success

stopped families should remain recoverable in the future

Failure modes:

stopping promising families too early

never stopping obviously bad families

What done looks like:
The engine can formally retire a bad intervention family for now.

21. New Content Freeze Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::stops::new_content_freeze

Role it plays:
Stop adding new material when regression is outpacing improvement.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

regression count

improvement count

strategy health

What it should produce:
A stop_new_content rule.

Why it exists:
This is the second audited stop-list category.

How to build it:
Start with the simple audited comparison: regression > improvement.
Then add exam-phase sensitivity later.

Invariants:

freeze must reflect actual negative movement, not mere caution

freeze should be scoped and reversible

Failure modes:

continuing to add content while the learner is losing ground

freezing too early on noisy data

What done looks like:
The engine can say “stop expanding; repair first.”

22. Diminishing-Return Family Stop Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::stops::diminishing_return_families

Role it plays:
Stop families that used to work but are now fading badly.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

diminishing returns alerts

family usage and recency

What it should produce:
Stop-family rules for fading approaches.

Why it exists:
This is the third audited stop-list category.

How to build it:
Trigger when diminishing returns detector fires above severity threshold.
Then attach a cooldown period before re-evaluation.

Invariants:

this stop is different from chronic ineffectiveness

historical success must be visible in the reason trace

Failure modes:

fading families kept alive too long

historically good families stopped without recent collapse evidence

What done looks like:
The engine can retire an approach because it is wearing out, not because it was never good.

23. Over-Review Stop Detector
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::stops::over_reviewing

Role it plays:
Stop the system from spending too much time on already-stable topics while stalled topics exist.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

stable-topic review load

stalled-topic count

review allocation patterns

What it should produce:
Stop-over-review rules.

Why it exists:
This is the fourth audited stop-list category.

How to build it:
Compare recent stable-topic review volume against stalled-topic pressure.
Then create a stop rule when imbalance exceeds threshold.

Invariants:

stable review is not bad by itself; it becomes bad when stalled topics are neglected

rule must be relative, not absolute only

Failure modes:

starving healthy maintenance entirely

never correcting clear review imbalance

What done looks like:
The engine can redirect attention from safe repetition back to urgent repair.

24. Recommendation Filter
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::recommendations::filter

Role it plays:
Filter adaptation recommendations when the system is over-adapting or when urgency is low.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

trigger hits

mode proposals

pacing report

stop list

learner risk context

What it should produce:
A filtered adaptation recommendation set.

Why it exists:
The audited engine explicitly filters recommendations by urgency when over-adapting, keeping only urgency > 0.75.

How to build it:
Implement the audited urgency filter first.
Then add conflict resolution against stop rules.

Invariants:

filtered-out recommendations must remain logged for audit

high-urgency protections should survive filtering

Failure modes:

low-urgency churn still leaking through

urgent adaptations dropped incorrectly

What done looks like:
Only the adaptations worth acting on reach the planner.

25. Adaptation Decision Composer
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::recommendations::composer

Role it plays:
Compose the final adaptation decisions after filtering.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:

strategy health

trigger hits

mode proposals

velocity report

learner profile

systemic patterns

stop list

What it should produce:
An AdaptationDecisionSet:

selected mode(s)

what changed

previous strategy

new strategy

urgency

rationale

Why it exists:
The audited engine persists adaptation decisions with fields like mode, trigger reason, what changed, previous/new strategy, and tension at time.

How to build it:
Start by allowing one primary adaptation per cycle.
Then add secondary non-conflicting recommendations later.

Invariants:

every decision must state previous vs new strategy

every decision must point to its trigger and evidence

Failure modes:

vague “adjust strategy” outputs

multiple incompatible changes in one cycle

What done looks like:
The planner gets a compact, explicit change package instead of raw diagnostics.

26. Adaptation Log Writer
Module path: adaptation_intelligence::persist::log_writer

Role it plays:
Persist final adaptation decisions.

Belongs to main engine: Adaptation Intelligence

What it should read:
Final adaptation decision set.

What it should produce:
ic_adaptation_log rows:

adaptation id

mode

trigger reason

what changed

previous strategy

new strategy

tension at time

Why it exists:
The planner already reads ic_adaptation_log for protect/compress/reroute signals. 


How to build it:
Persist one canonical row per adaptation decision cycle.
Then add related child tables later if richer history is needed.

Invariants:

every log row must include the specific mode and trigger reason

writes must be idempotent within one recompute cycle

Failure modes:

planner reading incomplete adaptation rows

adaptation history too vague to learn from

What done looks like:
The whole system can query adaptation history as one trustworthy ledger.

27. Build order for the Adaptation family
Adaptation sits at the top of the intelligence stack. It depends on topic cases, intervention outcomes, tensions, mission state, coaching episodes, and its own history. It also produces planner-facing signals like protect, compress, and reroute.

Recommended build sequence
Strategy Health Analyzer

Intervention Effectiveness Reporter

Trigger Evaluator

Trigger-to-Mode Mapper

Velocity Analyzer

Diminishing Returns Detector

Systemic Pattern Detector

Family Systemic Failure Detector

Subject Regression Wave Detector

Systemic Speed Deficit Detector

Systemic Recall Decay Detector

Learner Adaptive Profile Builder

Session Duration Analyzer

Pressure Response Analyzer

Learns-From-Mistakes Detector

Confidence Scaffolding Need Estimator

Teaching Mode Preference Builder

Adaptation Pacing Guard

Stop List Builder

Ineffective Family Stop Detector

New Content Freeze Detector

Diminishing-Return Family Stop Detector

Over-Review Stop Detector

Recommendation Filter

Adaptation Decision Composer

Adaptation Log Writer

Why this order
It follows the audited adaptation flow:

compute strategy health

compute family effectiveness

evaluate triggers

compute velocity and diminishing returns

detect systemic patterns

build learner profile

enforce pacing/dwell

build stop list

filter by urgency if over-adapting

persist decisions

What the whole Adaptation family should look like when finished
By the end, Adaptation Intelligence should behave like a real meta-learning layer:

it knows whether the current strategy is still healthy

it knows which intervention families are working, fading, or failing

it detects when local failure has become a systemic problem

it learns concrete traits about this learner, not generic averages

it can say when the system should protect, compress, reroute, reopen diagnosis, or simply refine

it prevents over-adapting through dwell rules

it explicitly says what to stop doing, not just what to try next

it logs every change in a way the planner and later analytics can trust

At this point, the six parent-engine registry series is complete. The strongest next step is a Cross-Engine Orchestration Pack:

engine event graph

recompute order

conflict resolution rules

planner precedence

shared fabric ownership

end-to-end execution traces


proceed

I’m moving up one level now from the six engine families into the glue that makes them behave like one brain: event flow, recompute order, conflict handling, and shared ownership. This is the layer that stops a strong set of engines from acting like six separate systems.

Thought for 27s
Below is the Cross-Engine Orchestration Pack.

This is the layer that turns your six audited engines into one coherent brain instead of six smart islands. Right now the audit already shows real cross-engine coupling: Topic writes ic_topic_teaching, ic_intervention_history, ic_topic_evidence_points, and delayed-recall timing signals; Coverage reads Topic signals; Timing, Risk, and Adaptation all read intervention history; and the study planner already reads Risk, Adaptation, and Timing outputs to alter ranking, compress plans, and even stop new learning. The current stack is therefore already a connected system, not a set of isolated modules. 

 


Cross-Engine Orchestration Pack
1. Orchestration mission
The orchestration layer exists to answer one question:

Given that multiple engines now know something important, what should recompute, in what order, under what guarantees, and which truth should win when engines disagree?

Without this layer, your engine family will suffer from:

stale downstream reads

duplicated recomputation

planner contradictions

race conditions between engine writes

unstable user-facing decisions

“smart locally, incoherent globally” behavior

2. The six orchestration responsibilities
2.1 Event routing
Every engine should not poll everything all the time. Orchestration routes events to the minimum valid recompute set.

2.2 Dependency scheduling
Some engines must run before others because their outputs are upstream truths.

2.3 Context assembly
Before an engine runs, orchestration should gather the right view of shared state and owned outputs.

2.4 Conflict resolution
When engines disagree, orchestration must decide which engine’s truth outranks the others.

2.5 Planner compilation
Planner should not read raw engine internals. Orchestration should hand it one consolidated decision bundle.

2.6 Auditability
Every recompute cycle should leave a trace:

what triggered it

which engines ran

what changed

which conflicts were resolved

why the final plan changed

3. Orchestration architecture
I would implement orchestration as its own family, not bury it inside Planner.

/intelligence-os
  /crates
    /intel-domain
    /intel-events
    /intel-store
    /intel-runner
    /intel-orchestrator
    /coach-planner
intel-orchestrator should own:
event-to-engine routing

dependency graph

recompute planner

conflict resolution

shared fabric hydration

engine cycle audit traces

stale snapshot invalidation

planner input bundle assembly

4. Shared fabrics and ownership
The six engines should not all own overlapping truths. Shared fabrics should exist, but ownership must still be precise.

4.1 Learner State Fabric
Primary owner: Adaptation
Primary readers: Topic, Timing, Sequencing, Risk, Planner

Why: Adaptation is already the engine that learns preferred families, timed response, confidence scaffold need, session duration patterns, and topic-type mode preferences. 


4.2 Evidence Fabric
Primary owner: Topic
Primary readers: Topic, Risk, Adaptation, Timing

Why: Topic already normalizes coaching steps into ic_topic_evidence_points and builds the evidence spine from them. 


4.3 Coverage Fabric
Primary owner: Coverage
Primary readers: Planner, Timing, Risk, Sequencing

Why: Coverage already owns bucketing, gaps, review obligations, demand, time budget, and projected readiness. 


4.4 Sequence Fabric
Primary owner: Sequencing
Primary readers: Planner, Risk, Timing

Why: Sequencing already owns the chosen model, alternatives, pivots, order, and stability/churn state. 


4.5 Timing Fabric
Primary owner: Timing
Primary readers: Planner, Topic, Risk

Why: Timing already owns reviews, retests, phase transitions, start-timed-work, run-mock, and stop-new-learning timing decisions. 


4.6 Risk Fabric
Primary owner: Risk
Primary readers: Planner, Timing, Adaptation

Why: Risk already owns topic/subject/phase risk rows, early warnings, protection rules, trend, and priorities. 


4.7 Adaptation Fabric
Primary owner: Adaptation
Primary readers: Planner, Topic, Sequencing, Timing

Why: Adaptation already owns protect/compress/reroute-style strategic changes plus learner-specific strategy memory. 


5. The dependency graph
This is the minimum engine-order truth.

5.1 Primary dependency chain
Topic → Coverage / Timing / Risk / Adaptation → Planner

That is already visible in the audit:

Topic writes ic_topic_teaching, which Coverage reads for proof contracts and evidence spines

Topic writes ic_intervention_history, which Timing reads for post-repair retests, Risk reads for intervention failure, and Adaptation reads for family effectiveness

Timing, Risk, and Adaptation all write planner-relevant outputs

Study planner reads ic_risk_assessments, ic_adaptation_log, and ic_timing_decisions and applies ranking and control effects. 


5.2 Secondary dependency chain
Coverage + Topic → Sequencing

Why: sequence model choice should be informed by topic truth and coverage posture, not just raw profile snapshots.

5.3 Timing/Risk/Adaptation cross-influence
These three are siblings, not a strict chain.

Timing may read intervention history and topic state

Risk may read timing context and sequence instability

Adaptation may read risk and timing outputs as change triggers

But none of them should own the other’s truth.

6. Event taxonomy
The orchestration layer should route around a strict event set.

pub enum TriggerEvent {
    QuestionAnswered,
    SessionCompleted,
    TopicRetestCompleted,
    DelayedRecallCompleted,
    TopicDecisionUpdated,
    CoverageClassificationUpdated,
    SequenceDecisionUpdated,
    TimingDecisionUpdated,
    RiskAssessmentUpdated,
    AdaptationDecisionUpdated,
    ExamProximityChanged,
    CurriculumUpdated,
    PlannerRequested,
    SystemRecompute,
}
Event classes
Hot events
Need fast recompute because learner just did something:

QuestionAnswered

SessionCompleted

TopicRetestCompleted

DelayedRecallCompleted

Warm events
Need selective recompute because one engine’s output changed:

TopicDecisionUpdated

CoverageClassificationUpdated

SequenceDecisionUpdated

TimingDecisionUpdated

RiskAssessmentUpdated

AdaptationDecisionUpdated

Cold events
Need broad recompute:

ExamProximityChanged

CurriculumUpdated

SystemRecompute

7. Recompute strategies by trigger
7.1 On QuestionAnswered
Run only if the question belongs to an active coached topic.

Order

Topic evidence ingestion

Topic local refresh

If topic state materially changed:

Timing micro refresh

Risk micro refresh

Adaptation micro refresh

Planner lightweight refresh

Why: Topic owns question-level interpretation first. Timing/Risk/Adaptation only react to changed topic truth.

7.2 On SessionCompleted
This is the most important hot path.

Order

Topic clinic feedback loop closure

Timing refresh

Risk refresh

Adaptation refresh

Coverage refresh if topic movement affects bucket/gap/demand materially

Sequencing refresh if bottleneck/urgency/churn inputs materially changed

Planner full refresh

This ordering fits the audited integration: Topic writes intervention history and delayed-recall timing signals; Timing, Risk, and Adaptation read those outputs; Coverage reads Topic truth; Planner reads Risk, Adaptation, and Timing outputs. 


7.3 On ExamProximityChanged
Order

Timing refresh

Coverage refresh

Risk refresh

Sequencing refresh

Adaptation refresh if strategy compression/triage trigger thresholds were crossed

Planner full refresh

Why: exam distance changes temporal truth first, then coverage realism, then danger, then order.

7.4 On CurriculumUpdated
Order

Topic invalidation for affected topics

Coverage full refresh

Sequencing refresh

Risk refresh if importance/coverage support changed

Planner refresh

Why: curriculum shifts alter the knowledge surface before anything else.

7.5 On RiskAssessmentUpdated
Order

Planner refresh immediately

Adaptation refresh only if protection or rising-fast thresholds crossed

Timing refresh if risk implies stop-new-learning or block-timed-work

Why: planner is the first consumer of danger truth. The audit already shows risk score and protection flags directly modify planner ranking. 


7.6 On AdaptationDecisionUpdated
Order

Planner refresh immediately

Topic refresh only if reopen_diagnosis

Sequencing refresh if sequence_shift

Timing refresh if timing_shift or compress

Coverage refresh only if new-content freeze or over-review stop materially changes allocation

Why: adaptation is a meta-decision layer. It should not force global recompute unless the mode implies it.

8. Recompute classes
To avoid expensive full-engine reruns constantly, orchestration should support three recompute levels.

8.1 Micro refresh
Affect one topic and direct dependents only.

Used for:

question answered

single retest

single delayed recall result

8.2 Subject refresh
Affect all topics in one subject.

Used for:

sequencing changes

subject exam changes

subject regression waves

coverage shifts

8.3 Global refresh
Affect learner-wide plan.

Used for:

curriculum update

system recompute

major strategy reset

major exam-calendar shift

9. Conflict resolution rules
This is the most important orchestration logic.

Once all six engines are deeper, they will disagree.

Example:

Coverage says a topic is high-yield priority

Sequencing says don’t study it yet because of interference spacing

Timing says not today because cooldown is active

Risk says block timed work

Adaptation says reopen diagnosis

Topic says proof is incomplete but confidence is fragile

Someone must win.

9.1 Precedence stack
I recommend this fixed order:

1. Protection truth
Risk protections outrank everything else.

Examples:

stop new learning

block timed work

isolate confusion pair

urgent protect topic

Reason: safety before optimization.

2. Readiness truth
Timing readiness outranks topic ambition.

Examples:

not ready for pressure mode

cooldown active

rest recommended

phase rollback

Reason: even the right task at the wrong time is wrong.

3. Validity truth
Topic proof and diagnosis truth outrank coverage optimism.

Examples:

proof blocked

no dominant hypothesis yet

must diagnose first

false mastery suspect

Reason: do not plan on illusions.

4. Structural truth
Sequencing constraints outrank raw urgency ordering.

Examples:

interference separation

bottleneck-first structural repair

sequence lock

Reason: bad order can corrupt otherwise good work.

5. Portfolio truth
Coverage allocation outranks local convenience.

Examples:

too much maintenance

time deficit requires triage

over-investment on stable topics

Reason: subject-wide viability matters.

6. Meta-strategy truth
Adaptation changes should modify but not bypass the five layers above.

Examples:

compress

reroute

reopen diagnosis

protect

refine

Reason: adaptation is the optimizer of the strategy, not the destroyer of safety constraints.

10. Planner precedence contract
The audit already shows concrete planner integration:

risk score > 0.6 gives +0.12 priority

active protection +0.08

adaptation protect +0.10

adaptation reroute -0.05

retest due today +0.15

compress mode reduces estimated sessions by 30% and drops low-yield-deferrable topics

stop new learning sets a flag on all topics. 


That should be formalized as a precedence contract.

10.1 Hard planner gates
These are non-negotiable:

stop_new_learning

block_timed_work

must_reopen_diagnosis

rest_required

cooldown_required

If one of these is active, planner cannot override it with urgency.

10.2 Soft planner modifiers
These affect ranking but do not hard-block:

risk boost

protection boost

adaptation protect boost

retest boost

compress reduction

reroute deprioritization

10.3 Planner output classes
Planner should always return:

do now

do soon

blocked

deferred

conditional on readiness

conditional on diagnosis

That lets orchestration expose why a high-yield topic did not make “do now.”

11. Orchestration sub-engines
I would build orchestration itself as a family.

11.1 Event Router
Routes events to the minimum engine set.

11.2 Dependency Scheduler
Knows engine order per trigger.

11.3 Context Hydrator
Loads shared fabrics and latest owned outputs before engine execution.

11.4 Engine Cycle Manager
Runs one recompute cycle with trace id, idempotency key, and retries.

11.5 Conflict Resolver
Applies the fixed precedence stack when engine truths collide.

11.6 Planner Input Compiler
Converts all engine outputs into one planner bundle.

11.7 Snapshot Invalidation Manager
Marks stale downstream snapshots when an upstream truth changed materially.

11.8 Audit Trace Writer
Writes:

trigger

engines run

outputs changed

conflicts resolved

final plan diff

12. Idempotency and write discipline
Without this, event-driven orchestration will become brittle fast.

12.1 One-cycle idempotency key
Use:
(learner_id, subject_id?, topic_id?, trigger_type, trigger_source_id, cycle_version)

12.2 Engine write rule
Each engine should only write its owned tables.

12.3 Outbox rule
Engine writes commit first, then orchestration emits downstream events from an outbox row.

12.4 Cycle dedupe
If the same trigger is replayed, orchestration should not create duplicate downstream writes.

13. Material-change gates
Do not recompute everything on every change.

13.1 Topic → Coverage recompute only if:
mastery state changed

proof blocked status changed

false mastery changed

bucket-driving readiness/fragility/importance changed materially

13.2 Topic → Sequencing recompute only if:
bottleneck changed

urgency rank changed materially

confusion/interference status changed

large readiness shifts changed order economics

13.3 Topic → Timing recompute only if:
delayed recall obligation changed

proof phase changed

recent improvement/regression changed retest/review logic

13.4 Topic → Risk recompute only if:
class-driving inputs moved materially

intervention failure changed

false mastery/regression/interference signals changed

13.5 Topic → Adaptation recompute only if:
new intervention outcome exists

topic entered stalled/regressing/protected state

learner profile inputs changed

This keeps orchestration efficient and prevents churn.

14. Engine-cycle audit record
Every orchestration cycle should persist something like:

pub struct EngineCycleAudit {
    pub cycle_id: Uuid,
    pub trace_id: Uuid,
    pub trigger: String,
    pub learner_id: Uuid,
    pub subject_id: Option<Uuid>,
    pub topic_id: Option<Uuid>,
    pub engines_run: Vec<String>,
    pub outputs_changed: Vec<String>,
    pub conflicts_resolved: Vec<ConflictResolutionRecord>,
    pub planner_diff: PlannerDiffSummary,
    pub started_at: DateTime<Utc>,
    pub finished_at: DateTime<Utc>,
}
This is crucial because once the system reaches 100k+ lines, debugging without cycle-level traces will become miserable.

15. End-to-end hot path example
SessionCompleted on a fragile misconception-heavy topic
SessionCompleted arrives

Event Router sends it to Topic first

Topic:

ingests evidence

rebuilds evidence spine

reruns hypotheses

updates proof

writes intervention outcome

requests delayed recall if needed

Snapshot Invalidation Manager marks:

Coverage stale if proof/bucket-driving truth changed

Timing stale if retest/review truth changed

Risk stale if class-driving features changed

Adaptation stale if family-effectiveness or topic-case state changed

Dependency Scheduler runs:

Timing

Risk

Adaptation

If Topic moved from non-bottleneck to bottleneck or changed confusion status materially:

Sequencing runs

Planner Input Compiler gathers:

latest topic truths

latest risk

latest adaptation

latest timing

latest coverage if changed

latest sequencing if changed

Conflict Resolver applies:

protections first

timing readiness second

topic validity third

sequencing constraints fourth

coverage portfolio fifth

adaptation sixth

Planner compiles final action plan

Audit Trace Writer records the cycle

That is what a real coach brain feels like operationally.

17. The most important rule
Do not let Planner become the hidden orchestrator.

Planner should choose what the learner does next.

Orchestration should decide:

what had to rerun

what truths are current

which truths outrank others

what bundle Planner is allowed to see

That separation will save you from a huge architecture mess later.

Bottom line
Your audit already proves the engines are meaningfully connected: Topic feeds Coverage, Timing, Risk, and Adaptation; Risk, Adaptation, and Timing directly affect the planner; and the current planner already applies concrete boosts, compressions, reroutes, and stop-new-learning flags from those engines. 


The orchestration layer is the missing operating system that will make those six engines behave like one intelligent coach.

The next strongest document would be a Full Engine Event Graph + Rust module contract pack, where I define:

exact events

exact recompute maps

exact orchestrator structs/traits

exact planner bundle schema

and exact conflict-resolution enums.


proceed

I’m turning the orchestration layer into a true implementation pack now: exact events, engine subscriptions, recompute maps, planner bundle shape, and conflict-resolution enums. This is the layer that makes the whole stack executable as one brain rather than a collection of rich specs.

Thought for 19s
Good. Here is the Full Engine Event Graph + Rust Module Contract Pack.

This pack is built on the current audited reality, not a hypothetical rewrite. Right now the stack already works as a connected system: Topic writes ic_topic_teaching, ic_intervention_history, ic_topic_evidence_points, and delayed-recall timing decisions; Coverage reads Topic signals; Timing, Risk, and Adaptation all read intervention history; Sequencing reads topic/profile/graph/history state; and study_plan.rs already queries ic_risk_assessments, ic_adaptation_log, and ic_timing_decisions to change ranking, compress plans, deprioritize reroutes, boost retests, and even stop new learning.

pub enum EngineEvent {

2. Engine subscription map

pub struct EngineSubscriptionMap;

impl EngineSubscriptionMap {
    pub fn subscribers(event: &EngineEvent) -> &'static [EngineName] {
        match event {
            EngineEvent::QuestionAnswered { .. } => &[
                EngineName::Topic,
            ],
            EngineEvent::SessionCompleted { .. } => &[
                EngineName::Topic,
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Adaptation,

                EngineName::Sequencing,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],
            EngineEvent::TopicRetestCompleted { .. } => &[
                EngineName::Topic,
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Adaptation,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],
            EngineEvent::DelayedRecallCompleted { .. } => &[
                EngineName::Topic,
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Adaptation,
                EngineName::Planner,

            EngineEvent::TopicDecisionUpdated { .. } => &[
                EngineName::Coverage,
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Adaptation,
                EngineName::Sequencing,
                EngineName::Planner,

3. Engine dependency graph
This is the authoritative execution order.

pub enum EngineName {
    Topic,
    Coverage,
    Sequencing,
    Timing,
    Risk,
    Adaptation,
    Planner,
}
pub struct EngineDependencyGraph;

impl EngineDependencyGraph {
    pub fn order_for(event: &EngineEvent) -> Vec<EngineName> {
        match event {
            EngineEvent::QuestionAnswered { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Topic,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::SessionCompleted { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Topic,
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Adaptation,
                EngineName::Coverage,
                EngineName::Sequencing,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::TopicRetestCompleted { .. }
            | EngineEvent::DelayedRecallCompleted { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Topic,
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Adaptation,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::TopicDecisionUpdated { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Coverage,
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Adaptation,
                EngineName::Sequencing,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::CoverageUpdated { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Sequencing,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::SequenceUpdated { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::TimingUpdated { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::RiskUpdated { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Adaptation,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::AdaptationUpdated { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Topic,
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Sequencing,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::ExamProximityChanged { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Coverage,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Sequencing,
                EngineName::Adaptation,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::CurriculumUpdated { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Topic,
                EngineName::Coverage,
                EngineName::Sequencing,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::PlannerRequested { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],

            EngineEvent::SystemRecompute { .. } => vec![
                EngineName::Topic,
                EngineName::Coverage,
                EngineName::Sequencing,
                EngineName::Timing,
                EngineName::Risk,
                EngineName::Adaptation,
                EngineName::Planner,
            ],
        }
    }
}
That ordering matches the current audited flow: Topic is the deepest source of local truth and writes the teaching decision, evidence, intervention history, and delayed-recall timing; Coverage consumes Topic signals; Timing, Risk, and Adaptation consume intervention and topic state; and the planner consumes Risk, Adaptation, and Timing outputs.

6. Hydrated shared state
Every engine should receive a prebuilt context, not open-coded table reads all over again.

pub struct HydratedState {

    pub coverage_state: Option<CoverageSnapshot>,
    pub sequence_state: Option<SequenceDecision>,

7. Material-change gates
This is critical to keep recompute cheap and stable.

pub enum MaterialChange {
    None,
    TopicStateChanged,
    ProofStateChanged,
    FalseMasteryChanged,
    BucketDriverChanged,
    BottleneckChanged,
    InterferenceChanged,
    ReviewOrRetestChanged,
    RiskDriverChanged,
    AdaptationDriverChanged,
}
pub struct MaterialChangeGate;

impl MaterialChangeGate {
    pub fn topic_to_coverage(diff: &TopicDiff) -> bool {
        diff.mastery_state_changed
            || diff.proof_blocked_changed
            || diff.false_mastery_changed
            || diff.readiness_delta.abs() >= 0.10
            || diff.fragility_delta.abs() >= 0.10
    }

    pub fn topic_to_sequencing(diff: &TopicDiff) -> bool {
        diff.bottleneck_changed
            || diff.urgency_rank_changed
            || diff.interference_changed
            || diff.readiness_delta.abs() >= 0.12
    }

    pub fn topic_to_timing(diff: &TopicDiff) -> bool {
        diff.delayed_recall_changed
            || diff.proof_phase_changed
            || diff.improvement_state_changed
            || diff.regression_state_changed
    }

    pub fn topic_to_risk(diff: &TopicDiff) -> bool {
        diff.false_mastery_changed
            || diff.regression_state_changed
            || diff.interference_changed
            || diff.intervention_failure_changed
    }

    pub fn topic_to_adaptation(diff: &TopicDiff) -> bool {
        diff.new_intervention_outcome
            || diff.stalled_state_changed
            || diff.regressing_state_changed
            || diff.learner_preference_signal_changed
    }
}
8. Planner input bundle
The planner should never read raw tables directly once orchestration exists. It should receive one consolidated bundle.

pub struct PlannerInputBundle {
    pub learner_id: LearnerId,
    pub subject_id: SubjectId,

    pub topic_decisions: Vec<TopicDecisionSummary>,
    pub coverage: Option<CoverageSnapshot>,
    pub sequencing: Option<SequenceDecision>,
    pub timing: Option<TimingDecisionSet>,
    pub risk: Option<RiskSummary>,
    pub adaptation: Option<AdaptationDecisionSet>,

    pub active_conflicts: Vec<EngineConflict>,
    pub trace_id: Uuid,
}
That fits the current planner behavior, which already consumes risk/adaptation/timing signals directly and applies boosts or hard flags such as retest-due-today and stop-new-learning. 


9. Conflict model
As the engines deepen, disagreement becomes normal, not exceptional.

pub enum ConflictKind {
    ProtectionVsUrgency,
    TimingVsCoverage,
    TimingVsTopic,
    SequencingVsCoverage,
    SequencingVsTopic,
    AdaptationVsCurrentPlan,
    RiskVsTimedMode,
    ProofVsAdvancement,
}
pub struct EngineConflict {
    pub kind: ConflictKind,
    pub left_engine: EngineName,
    pub right_engine: EngineName,
    pub subject_id: SubjectId,
    pub topic_id: Option<TopicId>,
    pub description: String,
}
10. Conflict precedence stack
This should be hard-coded and auditable.

pub enum ConflictPriority {
    Protection,
    Readiness,
    Validity,
    StructuralOrder,
    Portfolio,
    MetaStrategy,
}
Use this exact order:

Protection — Risk protections win first

Readiness — Timing readiness wins second

Validity — Topic proof/diagnosis truth wins third

StructuralOrder — Sequencing constraints win fourth

Portfolio — Coverage allocation truth wins fifth

MetaStrategy — Adaptation adjusts within the above five

That order fits the current engine responsibilities: Risk is the protective layer, Timing governs when, Topic governs proof and diagnosis, Sequencing governs ordering, Coverage governs portfolio balance, and Adaptation governs strategic change. 

 


11. Conflict resolver contract
pub trait ConflictResolver {
    fn resolve(
        &self,
        bundle: &PlannerInputBundle,
    ) -> ConflictResolutionSet;
}
pub struct ConflictResolutionSet {
    pub blocked_actions: Vec<BlockedAction>,
    pub deferred_actions: Vec<DeferredAction>,
    pub conditional_actions: Vec<ConditionalAction>,
    pub applied_resolutions: Vec<AppliedResolution>,
}
pub struct AppliedResolution {
    pub conflict: EngineConflict,
    pub winner: EngineName,
    pub priority: ConflictPriority,
    pub reason: String,
}
12. Hard planner gates
These must be enforced before ranking.

pub enum HardGate {
    StopNewLearning,
    BlockTimedWork,
    MustReopenDiagnosis,
    RestRequired,
    CooldownRequired,
}
Current audited planner behavior already effectively applies hard and soft controls: retest due today jumps priority, compress mode changes session volume and drops low-yield work, and stop-new-learning sets a flag on all topics. 


13. Soft planner modifiers
pub enum SoftModifierKind {
    RiskBoost,
    ProtectionBoost,
    AdaptationProtectBoost,
    AdaptationReroutePenalty,
    RetestBoost,
    CompressReduction,
}
pub struct SoftModifier {
    pub kind: SoftModifierKind,
    pub topic_id: TopicId,
    pub delta: f32,
    pub reason: String,
}
These should encode the current audited planner heuristics, including:

risk score > 0.6 => +0.12

active protection => +0.08

adaptation protect => +0.10

adaptation reroute => -0.05

retest due today => +0.15

compress => reduce estimated sessions by 30% and drop low-yield deferrables

stop new learning => hard gate.

        // 1. derive cycle_id + trace_id

        // 5. apply material-change gates
        // 6. collect outputs
        // 7. compile planner bundle
        // 8. resolve conflicts
        // 9. run planner
        // 10. write audit
        todo!()
    }

16. Idempotency
Every cycle must be replay-safe.

pub struct CycleIdempotencyKey {
    pub learner_id: LearnerId,
    pub subject_id: Option<SubjectId>,
    pub topic_id: Option<TopicId>,
    pub trigger_type: String,
    pub trigger_source_id: Option<Uuid>,
}
Use this to prevent duplicate runs for:

the same session completion

the same retest completion

the same delayed recall completion

17. Outbox pattern
Do not emit downstream events inline from random engines.

pub struct OutboxEvent {
    pub outbox_id: Uuid,
    pub cycle_id: Uuid,
    pub event: EngineEvent,
    pub emitted_by: EngineName,
    pub created_at: DateTime<Utc>,
}
Write owned tables first, then enqueue outbox events, then let orchestration fan them out. That keeps engine writes atomic and downstream recompute deterministic.

18. Snapshot invalidation
pub enum SnapshotKind {
    Coverage,
    Sequencing,
    Timing,
    Risk,
    Adaptation,
}
pub struct SnapshotInvalidation {
    pub kind: SnapshotKind,
    pub learner_id: LearnerId,
    pub subject_id: SubjectId,
    pub topic_id: Option<TopicId>,
    pub reason: String,
}
Examples:

Topic proof or false mastery change invalidates Coverage

Topic bottleneck/interference change invalidates Sequencing

Topic delayed recall or improvement state change invalidates Timing

Topic regression or intervention failure change invalidates Risk

Topic intervention outcome or stalled/regressing status change invalidates Adaptation

Thought for 31s
Below is the Database Ownership + Shared Fabric Schema Pack.

This pack locks your engine architecture to a durable data model. It is grounded in the current audited system, where Topic already writes ic_topic_teaching, ic_topic_evidence_points, ic_intervention_history, and delayed-recall timing decisions; Coverage writes ic_coverage_classification and ic_coverage_snapshots; Sequencing writes ic_sequencing_decisions and ic_sequencing_decision_topics; Timing writes ic_timing_decisions; Risk writes ic_risk_assessments; and Adaptation writes ic_adaptation_log. The audit also already shows the main cross-engine read paths and the current planner integration points. 

 


1. The database principle
The whole system should follow one rule:

each engine owns its truth, but shared fabrics expose reusable state without blurring ownership.

That means:

no two engines should co-own the same canonical table

downstream engines may read upstream outputs

downstream engines may cache derived views

only the owner engine can write canonical rows for that domain

This is the only way the architecture stays stable as the system grows.

2. Ownership map
2.1 Topic Intelligence owns
Canonical tables:

ic_topic_teaching

ic_topic_evidence_points

ic_intervention_history

Topic-originated delayed-recall requests in ic_timing_decisions

Why:
Topic is already the local reasoning engine and the audit confirms these are its actual write targets. It owns the full teaching decision, proof contract, evidence spine, normalized evidence, and clinic feedback loop output. 


2.2 Coverage Intelligence owns
Canonical tables:

ic_coverage_classification

ic_coverage_snapshots

Why:
Coverage already owns bucket assignments, portfolios, gap maps, review obligations, time-budget analysis, and readiness projection snapshots. 


2.3 Sequencing Intelligence owns
Canonical tables:

ic_sequencing_decisions

ic_sequencing_decision_topics

Why:
Sequencing already owns model choice, alternatives, pivots, and per-topic positions. 


2.4 Timing Intelligence owns
Canonical tables:

ic_timing_decisions

Why:
Timing already owns review actions, retest actions, phase transitions, start-timed-work, run-mock, and stop-new-learning decisions. 


2.5 Risk Intelligence owns
Canonical tables:

ic_risk_assessments

Why:
Risk already persists topic-, subject-, and phase-scope risk rows with upsert semantics. 


2.6 Adaptation Intelligence owns
Canonical tables:

ic_adaptation_log

Why:
Adaptation already writes mode, trigger_reason, what_changed, previous/new strategy, and tension_at_time, and the planner already consumes it. 


3. Shared fabrics
These are shared structures, not shared ownership.

3.1 Learner State Fabric
Owner: Adaptation
Primary purpose: persistent learner-specific strategy memory

Fields it should hold
learner readiness state

preferred intervention families

avoided families

average successful session length

pressure response profile

learns-from-mistakes signal

confidence scaffolding need

teaching mode preferences by topic type

Proposed canonical table
ic_learner_state

create table ic_learner_state (
  learner_id uuid primary key,
  readiness_state text not null,
  preferred_families jsonb not null default '[]'::jsonb,
  avoided_families jsonb not null default '[]'::jsonb,
  avg_successful_session_minutes integer,
  pressure_response jsonb,
  learns_from_mistakes_score real,
  confidence_scaffolding_need real,
  mode_preferences jsonb not null default '{}'::jsonb,
  evidence_confidence jsonb not null default '{}'::jsonb,
  updated_at timestamptz not null default now()
);
Why this table belongs to Adaptation
Adaptation is already the engine that learns family effectiveness, learner profile, pressure response, session duration patterns, and stop lists. 


3.2 Evidence Fabric
Owner: Topic
Primary purpose: normalized, reusable evidence truth

Canonical table
ic_topic_evidence_points

The audit already confirms this exists and is filled from ingest_coach_episode_evidence() with flags for transfer, timed, mixed_context, delayed_recall, and retrieval, including weight calculation and hint penalties. 


Recommended normalized shape
create table ic_topic_evidence_points (
  evidence_id uuid primary key,
  learner_id uuid not null,
  subject_id uuid not null,
  topic_id uuid not null,
  source_type text not null,
  source_id uuid,
  timed boolean not null default false,
  transfer boolean not null default false,
  mixed_context boolean not null default false,
  delayed_recall boolean not null default false,
  retrieval boolean not null default false,
  hints_used smallint not null default 0,
  correctness real not null,
  latency_ms bigint,
  representation_type text,
  misconception_tags jsonb not null default '[]'::jsonb,
  weight real not null,
  created_at timestamptz not null default now()
);
Read pattern
Topic reads it directly for evidence spine

Risk reads derived evidence summaries, not raw points unless necessary

Adaptation reads intervention outcomes more than raw evidence

Timing reads evidence indirectly through Topic outputs

3.3 Topic Decision Fabric
Owner: Topic
Primary purpose: canonical per-topic reasoning output

Canonical table
ic_topic_teaching

Recommended shape
create table ic_topic_teaching (
  learner_id uuid not null,
  subject_id uuid not null,
  topic_id uuid not null,
  decision_id uuid not null,
  dominant_hypothesis text,
  co_causes jsonb not null default '[]'::jsonb,
  teaching_mode text,
  entry_point text,
  mastery_state text,
  false_mastery_score real,
  bottleneck_concept_id uuid,
  evidence_spine jsonb not null,
  proof_contract jsonb not null,
  strategy_file jsonb not null,
  delayed_recall_required boolean not null default false,
  confidence_bundle jsonb not null,
  updated_at timestamptz not null default now(),
  primary key (learner_id, topic_id)
);
Why this matters
Coverage explicitly reads proof contracts, evidence spines, and false-mastery signatures from cached ic_topic_teaching rows.

);
Why this matters
Coverage already computes 7 buckets, 8 gaps, review obligations, session demand, budget, and projected readiness, then persists both per-topic and snapshot outputs.
